Divisive and Constitutive Roles of Differentiae in the Hierarchy
Author explains how differentiae act as divisors and constituents across genera and species, with exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between divisive and constitutive differentiae and identify the exceptions for highest genera and lowest species in a hierarchical classification.

Core Idea
Every differentia both divides the genus and constitutes the species, but not all divisive differentiae are constitutive.

In this passage, the author clarifies the dual role of differentiae: they act as divisors (muqassim) of genera and as constituents (muqawwim) of species. However, the relationship is not symmetric. Understanding these roles is essential for constructing accurate definitions in Islamic sciences, especially when dealing with hierarchical classifications such as in fiqh and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two roles of a differentia
A differentia both divides its genus (distinguishes one species from another) and constitutes the essence of its species. Note the terms 'fasl muqassim' (divisive differentia) and 'fasl muqawwim' (constitutive differentia).
2
Trace the hierarchy
Recognize that a genus can itself be a species of a higher genus (e.g., 'animal' is a species of 'living body'), and a species can be a genus for lower species. This nesting affects which differentiae are applicable at each level.
3
Apply the rule of reversal
Constitutive differentiae of a genus are also constitutive of its species (e.g., 'sensitive' constitutes animal and its species). Conversely, constitutive differentiae of a species are divisive of the genus (e.g., 'rational' divides animal into human and non-human). This rule does not reverse fully.
4
Handle exceptions
Highest genera (e.g., 'substance') have no constituent differentia because they have no genus above. Lowest species (e.g., 'human') have no divisive differentia because no species below. Accidents like 'laughing' can divide but not constitute, unless they define a subcategory (e.g., 'laughing animal'). Privations are divisive but not constitutive except in theoretical matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان الفصل مقسما للجنس ومقوما للنوع؛ والجنس للشيء قد يكون نوعًا لغيره، والنوع للشيء قد يكون جنسًا لغيره كذلك، فلا يمتنع أن يكون للجنس مقسم ومقوم وللنوع أيضًا مقسم ومقوم . وكل ما قوم الجنس كالحساس والمتحرك بالإرادة مثلا، فإنه مما يكون مقوما للحيوان قوم النوع ولا ينعكس، فإن ما يكون مقوما للنوع كالناطق للإنسان فلا يلزم أن يكون مقوما للجنس وهو الحيوان، ولا لجنس هذا الجنس، ولجنس جنسه كذلك. وكل ما قسم النوع كالصهيل مثلا ، فإنه مما يكون مقسما للحيوان إلى الفرس وغيره ; والحيوان نوع للجنس النامي كما مرَّ قسم الجنس، ولا ينعكس. فإن ما يكون مقسما للجنس كالحساس للجسم النامي فلا يلزم أن يكون مقسما للنوع وهو الحيوان ولا لنوع هذا النوع، ولنوع غيره كذلك. فالعوالي من الأجناس التحت لا مقوم لها وإلا لا تكون عالية، والسوافل من الأنواع التحت لا مقسم لها وإلا لا تكون سافلة، والمتوسطات مشتملة عليهما وإلا لا تكون أنواعا بالنسبة إلى ما فوقها من الأجناس، وأجناسا بالنسبة إلى تحتها من الأنواع، فلها فصول مقسمة ومقومة كذلك. وبالجملة فالفصول المقومة للأجناس مقومة للأنواع كلا وجملة، والفصول المقومة للأنواع مقسمة للأجناس كذلك. وقد يقال في هذا المقام لا يلزم أن تكون الفصول المقسمة للأجناس مقومة لأنواعها، فإن من الأعراض ما يكون مقسما للجنس كالضاحك مثلا، وإنه لا يكون مقوما البتة. وكذلك من الأعدام فإن منها ما يكون مقسما غير مقوم لكن الأول منها في حيز المنع; إذ الشيء لا يكون مقسما للشيء إلا وأن يكون مقوما لأحد قسميه، فالضاحك لا يكون مقسما للحيوان إلى الضاحك وغير الضاحك إلا وأن يكون مقوما لأحد قسميه وهو الحيوان الضاحك; وأما أنه إنسان وغير إنسان فذلك أمر آخر. وكذلك الثاني; إذ العدم لا يمكن أن يكون مقوما للشيء إلا في الأمور الاعتبارية ولا كلام فيها; بل الكلام في الغير.
And since the differentia is a divisor of the genus and a constituent of the species, and the genus of something may be a species for another, and the species of something may be a genus for another likewise, it is not impossible that the genus has a divisor and a constituent, and the species also has a divisor and a constituent. And everything that constitutes the genus, like 'sensitive' and 'moving by will' for example, is that which constitutes the animal, constitutes the species and does not reverse. For what constitutes the species, like 'rational' for man, does not necessarily constitute the genus, which is animal, nor the genus of this genus, and so on. And everything that divides the species, like 'neighing' for example, is that which divides the animal into horse and other; and animal is a species for the growing genus, as passed, divides the genus, and does not reverse. For what divides the genus, like 'sensitive' for the growing body, does not necessarily divide the species, which is animal, nor the species of this species, and so on. So the highest genera have no constituent, otherwise they would not be highest; and the lowest species have no divisor, otherwise they would not be lowest; and the intermediate ones comprise both, otherwise they would not be species relative to what is above them of genera, and genera relative to what is below them of species, so they have divisive and constitutive differentiae likewise. In summary, the differentiae constitutive of genera are constitutive of species wholly and completely, and the differentiae constitutive of species are divisive of genera likewise. It may be said at this point: it is not necessary that the differentiae that divide the genera be constitutive of their species, for among accidents there is what divides the genus, like 'laughing' for example, and it is not constitutive at all. And likewise among privations, for among them there is what is divisive but not constitutive. However, the first [case] is in the realm of prevention, for a thing does not divide a thing unless it is constitutive of one of its divisions. So 'laughing' does not divide the animal into laughing and non-laughing unless it is constitutive of one of its divisions, namely the laughing animal. As for it being human and non-human, that is another matter. And likewise the second, for privation cannot be constitutive of a thing except in theoretical matters, and there is no discussion about that; rather the discussion is about other things.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage examines the relationship between differentiae as divisors (muqassim) and constitutives (muqawwim) within the hierarchy of genera and species. The author begins by noting that since a differentia both divides the genus and constitutes the species, and since a genus can be a species for a higher genus, and a species can be a genus for lower species, it follows that both genera and species can have divisors and constituents. Then he gives examples: 'sensitive' and 'moving by will' are constitutive of the genus animal and also constitutive of its species, but this does not reverse: what is constitutive of a species (e.g., 'rational' for human) is not necessarily constitutive of its genus. Similarly, what divides a species (e.g., 'neighing' divides horse from other animals) also divides the genus, but not vice versa. He then discusses the highest genera (al-‘awali min al-ajnas) which have no constituent, and the lowest species (as-sawafil min al-anwa‘) which have no divisor, while intermediate categories have both. A summary statement: constitutive differentiae of genera are constitutive of species, and constitutive differentiae of species are divisive of genera. Finally, he addresses an objection concerning accidental differentiae like 'laughing' and privations, noting that these are divisive but not constitutive in a real sense; however, they can be constitutive in a derived sense (e.g., 'laughing' constitutes 'laughing animal' as a division of animal). Privations cannot be constitutive except in theoretical matters.

Key Terms
فصل مقسم
فصل مقوم
جنس عال
نوع سافل

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that the highest genera have no constituent differentia?
A) Because they have no genus above them
B) Because they are simple
C) Because they are not real
D) Because they are composed of accidents

Key Takeaway
Differentiae serve both to divide genera and to constitute species, but the relationship is not reversible, with exceptions for highest genera, lowest species, accidents, and privations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: In the definition of 'zakat' as 'a specified amount of wealth transferred to specific recipients under specific conditions', identify the genus and differentia. Is the differentia divisive, constitutive, or both? Explain based on the roles of differentiae.
?

Constitutive and Divisive Differentiae in the Hierarchy of Genera and Species
Defines rules of constitutive and divisive differentiate for genera and species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules of constitutive and divisive differentiae to any genus-species hierarchy in Islamic logical classification.

Core Idea
What constitutes the genus constitutes all species, but not vice versa.

The logical structure of genera and species is fundamental to precise definition. This passage explains how differentiae (fasl) relate to different levels of the hierarchy: some constitute essences, others divide genera. Understanding these rules will allow you to correctly classify entities and construct sound definitions in Islamic sciences like Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of differentiae
Every genus and every species can have both a constitutive differentia (muqawwim) that enters the essence, and a divisive differentia (muqassim) that separates it into lower kinds.
The passage uses 'constitutor' and 'divisor' as shorthand.
2
Trace the downward necessity
A differentia that constitutes a genus (like 'sensitive' for animal) necessarily constitutes all species under that genus (e.g., man, horse). But the reverse does not hold: a species-constitutive differentia (like 'rational') does not constitute the genus or higher genera.
Memorize: 'What constitutes the genus constitutes the species wholly and completely.'
3
Trace the upward divisibility
A differentia that divides a species (like 'neighing' for horse) divides the genus above it (animal into horse and non-horse). But a genus-divisive differentia (like 'sensitive' for growing body) does not necessarily divide the species under it.
The reverse is not symmetric: divisibility flows upward only from species to genus.
4
Apply to the hierarchy extremes
The highest genera (summum genus) have no constitutive differentia, otherwise they would not be highest – they would have a more basic genus. The lowest species (infima species) have no divisive differentia, otherwise they would have lower species. Middle terms have both.
This ensures the tree has proper roots (unconstituted) and leaves (undivided).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكون للجنس مقسم ومقوم وللنوع أيضًا مقسم ومقوم. وكل ما قوم الجنس كالحساس والمتحرك بالإرادة مثلا، فإنه مما يكون مقوما للحيوان قوم النوع ولا ينعكس، فإن ما يكون مقوما للنوع كالناطق للإنسان فلا يلزم أن يكون مقوما للجنس وهو الحيوان، ولا لجنس هذا الجنس، ولجنس جنسه كذلك. وكل ما قسم النوع كالصهال مثلا، فإنه مما يكون مقسما للحيوان إلى الفرس وغيره؛ والحيوان نوع للجنس النامي كما مرَّ قسم الجنس، ولا ينعكس. فإن ما يكون مقسما للجنس كالحساس للجسم النامي فلا يلزم أن يكون مقسما للنوع وهو الحيوان ولا لنوع هذا النوع، ولنوع غيره كذلك. فالعوالي من الأجناس التحت لا مقوم لها وإلا لا تكون عالية، والسوافل من الأنواع التحت لا مقسم لها وإلا لا تكون سافلة، والمتوسطات مشتملة عليهما وإلا لا تكون أنواعا بالنسبة إلى ما فوقها من الأجناس، وأجناسا بالنسبة إلى تحتها من الأنواع، فلها فصول مقسمة ومقومة كذلك. وبالجملة فالفصول المقومة للأجناس مقومة للأنواع كلا وجملة، والفصول المقومة للأنواع مقسمة للأجناس كذلك.
Every genus has a divisor and a constitutor, and every species also has a divisor and a constitutor. Whatever constitutes the genus, like 'sensitive' and 'voluntarily mobile' for example, it is something that constitutes the animal as a species, and it does not reverse. Indeed, whatever constitutes the species, like 'rational' for man, it does not necessitate that it constitutes the genus which is animal, nor the genus of this genus, and likewise for the genus of its genus. And whatever divides the species, like 'neighing' for example, it is something that divides the animal into horse and other; and animal is a species of the genus 'growing' as previously mentioned in the division of genus, and it does not reverse. Indeed, whatever divides the genus, like 'sensitive' for the growing body, it does not necessitate that it divides the species which is animal, nor the species of this species, and likewise for other species. Thus, the highest genera have no constitutor, otherwise they would not be highest; the lowest species have no divisor, otherwise they would not be lowest; and the middle ones include both, otherwise they would not be species relative to what is above them of genera, nor genera relative to what is below them of species, so they have divisive and constitutive differentiate accordingly. In summary, the differentiate that constitute genera constitute species wholly and completely, and the differentiate that constitute species divide genera likewise.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage defines the relationship between differentiate and the levels of genus and species. It begins by stating that every genus and every species has both a constitutive differentia (muqawwim) and a divisive differentia (muqassim). It then gives examples: 'sensitive' and 'voluntarily mobile' are constitutive of the genus 'animal' and therefore also constitute the species under 'animal'. However, the converse is not true: a differentia constitutive of a species, like 'rational' for man, does not necessarily constitute the genus 'animal' or higher genera. Similarly, a differentia that divides a species, like 'neighing' dividing 'animal' into horse and others, also divides the genus above that species. But a differentia that divides the genus, like 'sensitive' dividing 'growing body', does not necessarily divide the species. The passage then explains the hierarchy: the highest genera have no constitutive differentia (otherwise they would not be highest), the lowest species have no divisive differentia (otherwise they would not be lowest), and middle entities have both. Finally, the summary rule: constitutive differentiate of genera are constitutive of all species under them, while constitutive differentiate of species serve as divisive differentiate for their genera. This clarifies the precise role of differentiate in the Porphyrian tree.

Key Takeaway
Constitutive differentiae of genera flow downward to all species; constitutive differentiae of species only divide genera upward.

Why Every Divisive Differentia Must Be Constitutive
Author refutes objection about non-constitutive divisive differentiae

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why an accidental differentia like 'laughing' constitutes a species when dividing a genus, and refute the objection that accidents can be purely divisive.

Core Idea
No divisor without constitution: every divisive differentia must constitute one of its two species.

In this passage, the author confronts a challenge to a core rule of the five universals: that a divisive differentia for a genus must be constitutive for one of its species. The objector points to accidents like 'laughing' that seem to divide the genus 'animal' without constituting any species. The author's response is incisive: to be a divisor, 'laughing' must constitute one division, namely the 'laughing animal' (man). Let us unpack this argument and see how it preserves the integrity of logical definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objector claims that some accidents (like 'laughing') and privations can divide a genus without being constitutive of any species. Find this claim in the Arabic.
Look for 'لا يلزم أن تكون الفصول المقسمة للأجناس مقومة لأنواعها'
2
Examine the example of laughing
The objector uses 'laughing' as a divider of 'animal' into laughing and non-laughing animals. Note that laughing is an accident, not an essential property.
3
Follow the author's refutation
The author argues that 'laughing' cannot be a divisor unless it constitutes one of its divisions. It constitutes the 'laughing animal' (man). Thus it is constitutive after all.
The Arabic says: 'فالضاحك لا يكون مقسما للحيوان إلى الضاحك وغير الضاحك إلا وأن يكون مقوما لأحد قسميه وهو الحيوان الضاحك'
4
Apply the same logic to privations
Privations can only be constitutive in mental/conceptual matters, not in real definitions. So the objection fails for both types.
The passage says: 'العدم لا يمكن أن يكون مقوما للشيء إلا في الأمور الاعتبارية'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال في هذا المقام لا يلزم أن تكون الفصول المقسمة للأجناس مقومة لأنواعها، فإن من الأعراض ما يكون مقسما للجنس كالضاحك مثلا، وإنه لا يكون مقوما البتة. وكذلك من الأعدام فإن منها ما يكون مقسما غير مقوم لكن الأول منها في حيز المنع؛ إذ الشيء لا يكون مقسما للشيء إلا وأن يكون مقوما لأحد قسميه، فالضاحك لا يكون مقسما للحيوان إلى الضاحك وغير الضاحك إلا وأن يكون مقوما لأحد قسميه وهو الحيوان الضاحك؛ وأما أنه إنسان وغير إنسان فذلك أمر آخر. وكذلك الثاني؛ إذ العدم لا يمكن أن يكون مقوما للشيء إلا في الأمور الاعتبارية ولا كلام فيها؛ بل الكلام في الغير.
It may be said in this context: It does not follow that the differentiate that divide genera are constitutive of their species, for some accidents are divisors of the genus, like 'laughing' for example, and it is not constitutive at all. Likewise of privations, some are divisive but not constitutive. But the first [type – accidents] is subject to denial; for a thing cannot be a divisor of something unless it is constitutive of one of its two divisions. So 'laughing' is not a divisor of 'animal' into laughing and non-laughing unless it is constitutive of one of its divisions, namely the laughing animal. As for it being human and non-human, that is another matter. And similarly the second [type – privations]; for a privation cannot be constitutive of a thing except in mental matters, about which there is no discussion; rather the discussion is about other things.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes a potential objection to the earlier rule that a divisive differentia for a genus must be constitutive for one of its species. The objection says that some accidents (like 'laughing') divide a genus (animal) without being constitutive of any species—'laughing' is an accident, not a constitutive differentia. The author responds: this is false, because to be a divisor of a genus, the differentia must constitute one of the two divisions. In the case of 'laughing', it constitutes the species 'laughing animal' (i.e., man), even though it is an accident. Thus it does constitute a species. The objection about privations (al-'adam) is also dismissed: a privation cannot constitute anything except in conceptual matters (like in mathematics), which are not the subject of this logical discussion. Therefore, the original rule stands.

Key Takeaway
Every differentia that divides a genus must be constitutive of at least one species under that genus; otherwise it cannot be a divisor.

Why a Species Has Only One Differentia
Author proves uniqueness of differentia and distinguishes its three senses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the argument that a species has only one differentia and distinguish the three senses of 'differentia'.

Core Idea
One species, one differentia: the differentia is the cause of the species' particularity.

In this passage, the author tackles a common confusion: how can a species like 'animal' have two differentiate (sensitive and voluntary motion)? He explains that only one differentia constitutes a species, and apparent multiple differentiate combine into one composite differentia. He also clarifies the different meanings of 'differentia' in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The author asserts that a single differentia constitutes only one species. If a species had two differentiae at the same degree, it would not be more specific than either genus, which is impossible.
2
Understand the reductio
If two differentiae could combine with the same genus, the resulting species would not be uniquely determined; it would be ambiguous. Therefore, it's impossible.
The argument relies on the principle that the differentia is the cause of the species' particularity.
3
Address the animal objection
The author anticipates an objection: 'animal' seems to have two differentiate: sensitive and voluntarily mobile. He replies that the true differentia is the combination (المجموع) of these two, not each alone.
Neither sensitive alone nor voluntary motion alone constitutes animal; both together are needed.
4
Distinguish three senses of differentia
The author lists three meanings: (1) general differentia (فصل عام) – a distinguishing accidental attribute, possibly separable; (2) specific differentia (فصل خاص) – a necessary accident; (3) the most specific differentia (خاص الخاص) – what constitutes the species.
Only the most specific is the true constitutive differentia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الفصل الواحد لا يقوم إلا نوعًا واحدًا وإلا لا يمكن إقترانه بالجنسين، وذلك لا يمكن . فإنه إذا انضم كل واحد منهما لم يكن أخص من هذا الجنس مطلقا، ولا من ذلك كذلك، وذلك ممتنع لما مرّ من قبل؛ والفصل من جملة ما لا بد لكل نوع منه فيكون مقوما للنوع ومقسما لجنسه على ما عرف؛ والنوع من حيث هو النوع لا يمكن أن يتميز عن غيره من الأنواع بالفصل وغيره منه بالعدم؛ إذ الطبيعة الجنسية لا تكون خالية عن الفصل إلا وأن تكون غنية عنه والغنى عن الشيء لا يكون معلولاً له؛ فالخصة الموجودة في النوع من الجنس غنية عن الفصل ،إذن، وذلك باطل لما كان الفصل علة للخصة كما [۲۸] مرَّ . ثم الفصل في الدرجة الواحدة لا يكون إلا واحدا لما أن الفصل كمال الجزء المميز، وذلك لا يكون إلا واحدًا. ولا يقال كيف هو وقد كان للحيوان فصلان أحدهما حساس والآخر متحرك بالإرادة؛ إذ الفصل فيما نحن بصدده هو الذي يكون مقسما للجنس ومقوما للنوع كذلك، وذلك هو المجموع لا هذا بعينه، ولا ذلك أيضًا؛ فإن أحدهما بدون صاحبه لا يكون مقوما وإن كان من جملة ما يتميز به الشيء عن غيره . وقد مرّ من قبل أن الفصل قد يطلق على ما يتميز به الشيء عن غيره سواء كان ذلك من الأعراض المفارقة وهو الفصل العام أو اللازمة وهو الخاص، وعلى ما هو المقوم للنوع وهو خاص الخاص. ثم المشهور عندهم أن الماهية المركبة لا يمكن تركيبها إلا من جنس وفصل، وخالفهم الشيخ فيه لما أن الماهية المركبة قد تكون إلا من جنس وفصل نحو الجسم الأبيض مثلا، فإن البياض عرض غير مقوم للجسم والفصل مقوم ) . والحق في هذا البحث أن يكون مفتقرًا إلى البحث في الماهية أنها ما هي في اصطلاحهم، والحجة المشهورة على قولهم إن الماهية المركبة لا بد
Then a single differentia constitutes only one species; otherwise it could not be combined with two genera, and that is not possible. For if each one [of two differentiae] were attached, the species would not be more specific than this genus absolutely, nor than that one similarly, and that is impossible as previously mentioned. The differentia is among what every species necessarily requires, so it constitutes the species and divides its genus as known. The species qua species cannot be distinguished from other species by the differentia and other than it by privation; because the generic nature is not free of the differentia unless it is independent of it, and independence from a thing does not result from it. Therefore the particularity existing in the species from the genus is independent of the differentia – that is false because the differentia is the cause of the particularity as previously. Then the differentia at one degree is only one, because the differentia is the perfection of the distinguishing part, and that is only one. And do not say: How is that when animal had two differentiae, one sensitive and the other voluntarily mobile? For the differentia in our discussion is what divides the genus and constitutes the species, and that is the combination, not this one specifically nor that one either; for one of them without the other does not constitute, even though it is among what distinguishes a thing from others. And it has preceded that the differentia may be applied to what distinguishes a thing from others, whether that be from separating accidents – that is the general differentia – or from necessary accidents – that is the specific differentia – and to what constitutes the species, which is the most specific. Then the famous [view] among them is that the composite essence cannot be composed except from genus and differentia, but the Master [Ibn Sina] disagreed with them on this, because a composite essence may be composed from other than genus and differentia, like the white body for example: whiteness is an accident not constitutive of the body, and the differentia is constitutive. And the truth in this inquiry is that it requires investigation into the essence – what it is in their terminology – and the famous argument for their claim that the composite essence must...
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on differentiate, focusing on two main points: the uniqueness of the differentia for a species, and the composition of composite essence (mahiyya murakkaba). First, the author argues that a single differentia constitutes exactly one species. If a species had two differentiae at the same degree, it would not be more specific than either genus, leading to a contradiction. The differentia is essential to the species and causes its particularity. The species cannot be distinguished from others by the differentia and then by privation of the differentia, because the generic nature would then be independent of the differentia, contradicting the fact that the differentia is the cause of the particularity. Then the author addresses an objection: how can animal have two differentiae (sensitive and voluntarily mobile)? The answer is that the true differentia in this context is the combination of both, not each individually, because neither alone constitutes the species (e.g., sensitive without voluntary motion does not yield animal). The author distinguishes between different senses of 'differentia': (1) general differentia – a distinguishing accident that can be separating; (2) specific differentia – a necessary accident; (3) the most specific differentia – what constitutes the species. Finally, the author notes that the famous view is that composite essence is composed only of genus and differentia, but Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) disagreed, pointing to examples like 'white body' where the composition includes an accident (whiteness) alongside a differentia (body's own differentia). The passage ends mid-sentence on the truth of this matter, indicating further investigation is needed.

Ibn Sina's Objection to the Composition of Essence
Author reports Ibn Sina's objection to the exclusive composition of composite essence from genus and differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Ibn Sina's objection to the view that composite essence is composed only of genus and differentia, using the example of white body.

Core Idea
Composite essence may include accidents like whiteness, challenging the exclusive composition from genus and differentia.

In the latter part of the passage, the author reports a famous debate in Islamic logic: Is a composite essence always composed of genus and differentia? The common view says yes, but Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) disagrees, pointing to examples like 'white body' (جسم أبيض) where whiteness is an accident, not a differentia. This lesson explores the debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common view
The author states: 'المشهور عندهم أن الماهية المركبة لا يمكن تركيبها إلا من جنس وفصل' – the famous view is that composite essence can only be composed of genus and differentia.
2
Locate Ibn Sina's counterexample
Ibn Sina objects with 'الجسم الأبيض' (white body). In this composite, 'body' is the genus, but 'white' is an accident, not a differentia. Yet 'white body' is still a composite essence.
Whiteness is a qualifying accident, not a constitutive part of the essence 'body'.
3
Understand the reasoning
If composite essence must be from genus and differentia, then 'white body' should not be a composite essence – but it clearly is. Therefore, the common view is too restrictive.
4
Reflect on the resolution
The author suggests that the truth requires investigation into what 'essence' (mahiyya) means in their terminology. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating the debate continues.
Later logicians may respond by defining 'composite essence' more narrowly.

ثم الفصل الواحد لا يقوم إلا نوعًا واحدًا وإلا لا يمكن إقترانه بالجنسين، وذلك لا يمكن . فإنه إذا انضم كل واحد منهما لم يكن أخص من هذا الجنس مطلقا، ولا من ذلك كذلك، وذلك ممتنع لما مرّ من قبل؛ والفصل من جملة ما لا بد لكل نوع منه فيكون مقوما للنوع ومقسما لجنسه على ما عرف؛ والنوع من حيث هو النوع لا يمكن أن يتميز عن غيره من الأنواع بالفصل وغيره منه بالعدم؛ إذ الطبيعة الجنسية لا تكون خالية عن الفصل إلا وأن تكون غنية عنه والغنى عن الشيء لا يكون معلولاً له؛ فالخصة الموجودة في النوع من الجنس غنية عن الفصل ،إذن، وذلك باطل لما كان الفصل علة للخصة كما [۲۸] مرَّ . ثم الفصل في الدرجة الواحدة لا يكون إلا واحدا لما أن الفصل كمال الجزء المميز، وذلك لا يكون إلا واحدًا. ولا يقال كيف هو وقد كان للحيوان فصلان أحدهما حساس والآخر متحرك بالإرادة؛ إذ الفصل فيما نحن بصدده هو الذي يكون مقسما للجنس ومقوما للنوع كذلك، وذلك هو المجموع لا هذا بعينه، ولا ذلك أيضًا؛ فإن أحدهما بدون صاحبه لا يكون مقوما وإن كان من جملة ما يتميز به الشيء عن غيره . وقد مرّ من قبل أن الفصل قد يطلق على ما يتميز به الشيء عن غيره سواء كان ذلك من الأعراض المفارقة وهو الفصل العام أو اللازمة وهو الخاص، وعلى ما هو المقوم للنوع وهو خاص الخاص. ثم المشهور عندهم أن الماهية المركبة لا يمكن تركيبها إلا من جنس وفصل، وخالفهم الشيخ فيه لما أن الماهية المركبة قد تكون إلا من جنس وفصل نحو الجسم الأبيض مثلا، فإن البياض عرض غير مقوم للجسم والفصل مقوم ) . والحق في هذا البحث أن يكون مفتقرًا إلى البحث في الماهية أنها ما هي في اصطلاحهم، والحجة المشهورة على قولهم إن الماهية المركبة لا بد
Then a single differentia constitutes only one species; otherwise it could not be combined with two genera, and that is not possible. For if each one [of two differentiae] were attached, the species would not be more specific than this genus absolutely, nor than that one similarly, and that is impossible as previously mentioned. The differentia is among what every species necessarily requires, so it constitutes the species and divides its genus as known. The species qua species cannot be distinguished from other species by the differentia and other than it by privation; because the generic nature is not free of the differentia unless it is independent of it, and independence from a thing does not result from it. Therefore the particularity existing in the species from the genus is independent of the differentia – that is false because the differentia is the cause of the particularity as previously. Then the differentia at one degree is only one, because the differentia is the perfection of the distinguishing part, and that is only one. And do not say: How is that when animal had two differentiae, one sensitive and the other voluntarily mobile? For the differentia in our discussion is what divides the genus and constitutes the species, and that is the combination, not this one specifically nor that one either; for one of them without the other does not constitute, even though it is among what distinguishes a thing from others. And it has preceded that the differentia may be applied to what distinguishes a thing from others, whether that be from separating accidents – that is the general differentia – or from necessary accidents – that is the specific differentia – and to what constitutes the species, which is the most specific. Then the famous [view] among them is that the composite essence cannot be composed except from genus and differentia, but the Master [Ibn Sina] disagreed with them on this, because a composite essence may be composed from other than genus and differentia, like the white body for example: whiteness is an accident not constitutive of the body, and the differentia is constitutive. And the truth in this inquiry is that it requires investigation into the essence – what it is in their terminology – and the famous argument for their claim that the composite essence must...
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on differentiate, focusing on two main points: the uniqueness of the differentia for a species, and the composition of composite essence (mahiyya murakkaba). First, the author argues that a single differentia constitutes exactly one species. If a species had two differentiae at the same degree, it would not be more specific than either genus, leading to a contradiction. The differentia is essential to the species and causes its particularity. The species cannot be distinguished from others by the differentia and then by privation of the differentia, because the generic nature would then be independent of the differentia, contradicting the fact that the differentia is the cause of the particularity. Then the author addresses an objection: how can animal have two differentiae (sensitive and voluntarily mobile)? The answer is that the true differentia in this context is the combination of both, not each individually, because neither alone constitutes the species (e.g., sensitive without voluntary motion does not yield animal). The author distinguishes between different senses of 'differentia': (1) general differentia – a distinguishing accident that can be separating; (2) specific differentia – a necessary accident; (3) the most specific differentia – what constitutes the species. Finally, the author notes that the famous view is that composite essence is composed only of genus and differentia, but Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) disagreed, pointing to examples like 'white body' where the composition includes an accident (whiteness) alongside a differentia (body's own differentia). The passage ends mid-sentence on the truth of this matter, indicating further investigation is needed.

The Two Relations of Differentiae: Constitutive and Divisive
Author explains that differentiae both constitute species and divide genera, and refutes negative differentiae.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two relations of a differentia and distinguish true differentiae from negative ones.

Core Idea
Differentiae both divide genera and constitute species; negative differentiae are not genuine.

The differentia is a key universal in logic, serving a dual role: it divides the genus into species and constitutes the essence of a species. This passage clarifies that every true differentia has these two relations, and warns against mistaking accidental negations for real differentiae. Understanding this distinction is crucial for constructing sound definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two relations
The author states that every differentia has a relation to the genus (what it divides) and to the species (what it constitutes). Locate these in the text.
The differentia 'rational' divides 'animal' and constitutes 'human'.
2
Compare constitutive vs divisive
Constitutive elements of a genus are not more specific than the genus; divisive elements are more specific. Find the author's explanation of this difference.
3
Evaluate negative differentiae
The author argues that negative differentiae like 'non-rational' are not real differentiae because they are negations, not positive properties. How does the example of 'odd' (non-even) illustrate this?
Odd is a species defined by negation, but its differentia is not truly negative in a full definition.
4
Refute the combined differentia claim
Some argue that a differentia like 'sentient' becomes constitutive only when combined with another. Why does the author reject this?
The key point: a differentia constitutes only the species it defines, not another species.
5
Apply to 'rational animal' example
The author uses 'rational' as a differentia that constitutes 'rational animal' (a species of animal) and divides 'animal'. Verify this with the example of human and angel.
Both human and angel are rational animals, showing 'rational' is a true differentia.

ذلك إلى أن نتكلم في المباينات، وقد مرَّ من قبل أن كل فصل إنما يقوم من الأنواع نوعًا واحدا فقط، وله نسبتان نسبة إلى ما يقسمه وهو الجنس ونسبة إلى ما يقومه وهو النوع، فتكون الفصول مقومة للأنواع ومقسمة للأجناس، ومقومات الجنس لا تكون أخص منه على خلاف المقسمات، فإنها تكون أخص، وليس من الفصول المقومة ما لا يقسم، ومن الفصول المقسمة ما لا يقوم ولا يكون ذلك إلا من الفصول السلبية التي ليست بالحقيقة فصولاً كما في قولنا إن الحيوان منه ناطق وغير ناطق، فغير الناطق لا يكون نوعا محصلا بإزاء الناطق. اللهم إلا أن يتفق أن يكون ما ليس بناطق نوعا واحدا كالذي ليس بمقسم بمتساويين بحسب العدد، فإنه صنف واحد وهو الفرد. وكذلك في جميع أمثال هذه السُّلوب، فإن السلوب لوازم للأشياء بالقياس إلى اعتبار معاني ليست لها، وغير الناطق أمر يعقل باعتبار الناطق فلا تكون مقومة للأشياء من حيث هي سلوب؛ بل هي عوارض ولوازم إضافية. فلا يكون غير الناطق بالحقيقة فصله مقوما يشترك فيه الناطق وغيره، وما يكون من الفصول الحقيقية فلا يكون إلا مقوما. ومن زعم بأن من الفصول المقسمة ما لا يكون مقوما من حيث هو هو؛ بل يكون مقوما مع الغير، كما أن الحساس يقسم الجنس النامي ثم يتوقف في تقويم النوع إلى أن ينضم إليه المتحرك بالإرادة» فزعمه باطل، والكلام فيه قد مرّ، ولأنه ليس من شرط الفصل إذا كان مقسما للجنس ومقوما للنوع أن يكون مقوما للنوع الآخر. فإن الفرق بين بين أن يكون مقوما للنوع وبين أن يكون مقوما للنوع الآخر، والناطق وإن كان لا يقوم الإنسان الذي هو النوع الآخر فإنه يقوم الحيوان الناطق الذي هو نوع للحيوان وجنس للإنسان، إذا كان الناطق أعم من الإنسان كما ذهب إليه قوم؛ وقد مرَّ الكلام من قبل في أنه لا يمكن أن يكون أعم. ثم الإنسان حي ناطق والملك كذلك الحي الناطق يعم على الإنسان وعلى الملك لا بالاشتراك اللفظي؛ بل بالاشتراك المعنوي لما أن صح فالحي الناطق، وإن كان أخص من الحي فلا يمكن أن يكون فصلا له؛ بل الفصل جزء منه وهو الناطق، ولا خاصة أيضًا والخاصة مما يعرض على الحي الناطق فيكون نوعًا له لا محالة، فالناطق إذن يقوم نوعًا ويقسم جنسًا حينئذ، ولما صح على الإنسان أنه حيوان ناطق صح عليه أنه حي ناطق.
That leads to our speaking about differentiae. It has passed before that every differentia only constitutes one species only, and it has two relations: a relation to what it divides, which is the genus, and a relation to what it constitutes, which is the species. Thus differentiae are constitutive of species and divisive of genera. The constitutive elements of the genus are not more specific than it, unlike the divisive elements, for they are more specific. There is no constitutive differentia that does not divide, and among divisive differentiae there is that which does not constitute, and this only occurs from negative differentiae which are not in reality differentiae, as in our saying: 'Of animal, there is rational and non-rational,' so non-rational does not become a perfectly realized species corresponding to rational. Unless it happens that what is not rational is one species, like what is not divided by two equal [parts] in terms of number, for it is one kind, namely the odd. And likewise in all such negations. For negations are concomitants of things in relation to the consideration of meanings that they do not have, and 'non-rational' is something understood in terms of 'rational', so they are not constitutive of things insofar as they are negations; rather they are accidental and relational concomitants. So 'non-rational' is not in reality a differentia constituting something in which rational and other share. Whatever is from true differentiae is only constitutive. And whoever claims that among divisive differentiae there is that which is not constitutive as such, but becomes constitutive together with another, like how 'sentient' divides the genus 'growing' and then awaits the constitution of the species until 'voluntarily mobile' is joined to it — his claim is false. The discussion has passed, and because it is not a condition of the differentia, when it divides the genus and constitutes the species, that it constitutes the other species. For the difference between being constitutive of a species and being constitutive of another species. And 'rational', even if it does not constitute the human which is the other species, it constitutes 'rational animal' which is a species of animal and a genus of human, if 'rational' is more general than human as some have held. The discussion has passed before that it cannot be more general. Then human is a rational animal, and likewise angel; so 'rational animal' applies to human and angel not by verbal commonality but by meaning commonality, since it is valid. So 'rational animal', even if it is more specific than animal, cannot be a differentia for it; rather the differentia is a part of it, namely 'rational'. And there is no property either, and property is something that inheres in 'rational animal', so it becomes a species necessarily. Thus 'rational' then constitutes a species and divides a genus at that time. Since it is valid for human that he is a rational animal, it is valid that he is a rational animal.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage defines the two aspects of differentiae (فصول). The author begins by stating that each differentia constitutes only one species and has two relations: to the genus (the thing it divides) and to the species (the thing it constitutes). Therefore differentiae are constitutive of species and divisive of genera. The author then explains that constitutive elements of a genus are not more specific than the genus, unlike divisive elements which are more specific. He then discusses negative differentiae, such as 'non-rational', arguing that they are not true differentiae because they are negations and not real properties; they only appear as differentiae but are accidental. He gives the example of odd numbers as a species defined by negation. Next, he refutes the claim that some divisive differentiae are not constitutive alone but become constitutive when combined with another differentia, using the example of 'sentient' and 'voluntarily mobile'. He argues that this view is false because a differentia's role is not to constitute the other species; e.g., 'rational' constitutes 'rational animal' even if it does not constitute human directly. Finally, he concludes that 'rational' constitutes a species and divides a genus, using the example of human and angel being both rational animals. The passage emphasizes that true differentiae are always constitutive, and negative differentiae are not genuine.

Key Terms
فصل (fasl): A differentia that constitutes a species and divides a genus; it has two relations: divisive of genus and constitutive of species.
جنس (jins): The genus which is divided by differentiae and is the broader category.
نوع (naw‘): The species constituted by a differentia and is the subordinate category.
سلب (salab): Negation; negative differentiae are not true differentiae because they are not real properties but accidents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two relations of a differentia?
A) It divides the species and constitutes the genus.
B) It divides the genus and constitutes the species.
C) It divides the differentia and constitutes the accident.
D) It divides the property and constitutes the genus.

Key Takeaway
True differentiae always divide a genus and constitute a species, and they must be positive properties, not negations.

The Property (خاصّة) Among the Five Universals
Author defines الخاصة as a non-essential universal predicate specific to one species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of الخاصّة as one of the five universals and distinguish it from the other two meanings of the term.

Core Idea
خاصّة is the non-essential predicate belonging to all individuals of a species, whether always present or not.

In this lesson, we examine the fifth universal, الخاصّة (property). The author clarifies that the word ‘خاصّة’ has three possible meanings, and the one intended as a universal is the middle meaning—a non-essential predicate (قول غير ذاتي) that applies to all members of a single species, regardless of whether it is always present. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the five universals in logical definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three meanings of 'خاصّة'
The author lists: (1) specific to a thing absolutely or relatively, (2) specific to a species itself, and from that (3) always present in all members of the species.
2
Determine which meaning is the fifth universal
It is the middle meaning (the second in the list): a non-essential predicate answering 'what is it?' for individuals of one species, whether always present or not.
The first meaning would make the division exceed five; the third is too narrow.
3
Contrast with common accident (عرض عام)
خاصّة is specific to one species; common accident belongs to more than one species.
4
Verify the definition’s completeness
Check that this definition includes all properties (e.g., 'ability to laugh' for humans) and excludes common accidents (e.g., 'having two eyes').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في الخاصة ] وأما «الخاصة» فإنها من الأعراض ما يستعمل عند أهل المنطق على معنيين أيضًا : [أ] أحدهما أن يقال على معنى يخص شيئًا كان على الإطلاق أو بالقياس إلى شيء، [ب] وثانيهما أن يقال على معنى يخص شيئًا من الأنواع في نفسه دون الأشياء الأخرى. ثم من هذا القسم ما قد يخص باسم الخاصة وهو الذي يكون مع ذلك شيئًا موجودًا لكل النوع في كل الزمان. والخاصة التي هي قسم من الأقسام الخمسة هي الوسط من هذه الثلاثة، وهي المقول على الأشخاص من نوع واحد في جواب أي شيء هو قول غير ذاتي سواء كان عاما في كل وقت أو لم يكن . فإن العام الموجود في كل وقت هو أخص من هذا، ولو كانت الخاصة التي هي إحدى الخمسة هذه لكانت القسمة تزيد على الخمسة، وإن كان الأولى باسم الخاصة باعتبار اختصاصها بالنوع غيرها. ولا يبعد أن يعني بالخاصة كل عارض خاص لكلي أي كلي كان، والخاصة لا محالة في مقابلة العرض العام؛ وإنها إنما تحسن أن تصير خاصة؛ لأنها لا تكون إلا لنوع واحد. فالعام إنما يحسن أن يصير عاما؛ لأنه لا يكون إلا لأكثر من نوع واحد، فإذن ليس يحسن أن يجعل أخص الوجوه الثلاثة في استعمال اللفظ دالا على المعنى الذي هو أحد الأقسام الخمسة.
Section on Property. As for 'property', it is among the accidents that is used by logicians in two meanings as well: [A] One: it is said with a meaning that is specific to a thing either absolutely or in relation to something. [B] Second: it is said with a meaning that is specific to a species by itself without other things. Then from this division, there is that which is particularly called 'property', and it is that which exists for the whole species at all times. The property that is one of the five divisions is the middle of these three. It is what is predicated of the individuals of one species in answer to 'what is it?' as a non-essential statement, whether it be common in every time or not. For the common existing in every time is more specific than this. Had the property that is one of the five been this [the first meaning], the division would exceed five. Although the first is more deserving of the name 'property' due to its being specific to the species other than it. It is not far-fetched that by 'property' is meant every specific accident for any universal whatever. Property is necessarily opposite to common accident. And it only becomes fitting to be 'property' because it is only for one species. The common only becomes fitting to be 'common' because it is only for more than one species. Therefore it is not fitting to make the most specific of the three usages of the term indicate the meaning that is one of the five divisions.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth universal: الخاصة (property). The author explains that the term 'property' is used in two general meanings: one meaning is something specific to a thing absolutely or relatively, the second is something specific to a species in itself. From the second meaning, there is a further specification: a property that exists for all members of the species at all times. The property that is one of the five universals is the middle of these three: it is a non-essential predicate (قول غير ذاتي) that applies to all individuals of a species in answer to 'what is it?', whether it is common in every time or not. The author argues that if the property were the first meaning (absolute specificity), the classification of five universals would be exceeded. He clarifies that the first meaning is more deserving of the name 'property' because of its specificity, but the intended one is the middle. Property is opposite to common accident (عرض عام). It is fitting as property because it belongs only to one species, whereas common accident belongs to more than one species. Therefore, the most specific of the three usages should not be taken as the one intended among the five universals.

عرض

الخاصّة as a universal is a non-essential predicate that applies to all individuals of a single species, but may or may not be always present.

Defining al-Khassa: The Special Property as a Universal
Author defines al-khassa as one of the five universals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of al-khassa and distinguish it from other universals using its genus and differentia.

Core Idea
Al-khassa is a universal accident predicated of all individuals of one species in answer to 'what is it?'

The special property (al-khassa) is one of the five universals in Islamic logic. It captures non-essential but universal features of a species. In this lesson, we unpack its precise definition and see how it differs from genus, species, differentia, and general accident.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of khassa
Find the sentence that begins 'والخاصة أيضًا من جملة ما يعرف بتعريفات مختلفة' and ends with 'ويخرج الجنس والعرض العام'. This is the core definition.
The author offers two formulations; the second is preferred.
2
Identify the genus: universal accident
The definition says 'كلي عرضي' (universal accident). This places khassa in the category of accidents, not substances or essential properties.
3
Identify the differentia: predicated of individuals of one species in answer to 'what is it?'
The phrase 'مقول على الأشخاص من نوع واحد في جواب أي شيء هو' is the differentia. It specifies that khassa is predicated of all individuals of a single species and answers 'what is it?'
This answer is what the species is (like 'man'), not the definition.
4
Test the definition against other universals
The author shows how each part of the definition excludes other universals: 'كلي عرضي' excludes genus, species, differentia; 'مقول على الأشخاص من نوع واحد' excludes genus and general accident; 'في جواب أي شيء هو' excludes genus and differentia.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا الاستعمال الأعم يجعل الخواص مقسومة إلى أربعة أقسام خاصة للنوع ولغيره كذي الرجلين للإنسان بالنسبة إلى الفرس ولغيره كذلك خاصة لنوع وحده؛ وإنها إما لكله أو لا لكله، فما لكله فذلك قد يكون دائما وقد لا يكون. فالخاصة من حيث هو أولى أن تكون إحدى الخمسة هي ما ذكرناه، وأما من حيث هي أولى بأن تكون خاصة فهي اللازمة المداومة لجميع النوع في كل زمان والخاصة أيضًا من جملة ما يعرف بتعريفات مختلفة: فيقال في غير تعريف ما هو من الخمسة إنها كلية مقولة على جزئيات نوع واحد قولاً غير ذاتي، ويقال أيضًا إنها عبارة عن كلي عرضي مقول على الأشخاص من نوع واحد في جواب أي شيء هو وهذا هو الأولى وأحسن بالنسبة إلى الغير، فقولنا كلي عرضي يخرج الجنس والنوع والفصل، وقولنا مقول على الأشخاص من نوع واحد يخرج الجنس والعرض العام، وقولنا في جواب أي شيء هو يخرج الجنس والفصل والجنس أيضًا. وإنها أي الخاصة قد تكون مطلقة وهي التي لا توجد خارجة عن ذلك النوع كالضاحك مثلا، فإن التي هي من الخمسة هي الضحاك لا الضحك على ما قيل في الفصل، ولا يمتنع أن يؤخذ الضحك في الاستعمال مكان الضاحك، وقد لا تكون مطلقة بل بالإضافة وهي التي تؤخذ في بعض ما يخالف النوع دون البعض فيكون خاصة لذلك النوع بالنسبة إلى ما لم تؤخذ فيه. فيقال للجوهر مثلا إنه غير قابل للأشد والأضعف، وإنه خاصة له لا بالنسبة إلى الكل؛ بل بالنسبة إلى البعض. فإن من الأعراض ما يكون كذلك مثل الكمية، وقد مرّ من قبل ما يكون على مثال هذا أيضًا. ثم الخاصة قد تكون مساوية كالضاحك بالنسبة إلى الإنسان مثلا، وقد لا تكون؛ بل تكون أخص كالضاحك بالفعل، فتكون على ثلاثة أقسام بهذا الاعتبار لا محالة. لأنها [أ] إما أن تكون لازمة كالضاحك بالقوة، [ب] وإما لا تكون؛ بل تكون مفارقة كالضاحك بالفعل، والمفارقة إما أن تكون عامة تعم كل فرد من أفراد النوع نحو المتعجب بالفعل، وإما أن تكون نحو المتعصب بالفعل. وإنها قد تكون بسيطة كالضحك لازمة كانت البسيطة أو مركبة؛ وقد تكون مركبة، والمركبة قد تكون من الصفات الخاصة للنوع، وقد تكون من العامة، وقد تكون الخاصة للنوع والعامة على ما عرف من قبل، فاعتبر بما عرفت.
And this more general usage makes the special properties divided into four divisions: a special property for the species and for other than it, like 'possessor of two legs' for man relative to horse, and also for other than it, and a special property for a single species only; and it is either for the whole or not for the whole. What is for the whole may be permanent or not. So the special property insofar as it is more appropriate to be one of the five is what we mentioned, but insofar as it is more appropriate to be a special property, it is the concomitant enduring for the entire species at all times. The special property is also defined by various definitions: it is said in a non-definition of what is from the five that it is a universal predicated of the particulars of one species in a non-essential predication, and it is also said that it is an expression of a universal accident predicated of the individuals of one species in answer to 'what is it?', and this is more appropriate and better relative to others. So our saying 'universal accident' excludes genus, species, and differentia; our saying 'predicated of the individuals of one species' excludes genus and general accident; and our saying 'in answer to what is it?' excludes genus and differentia, and also genus. And the special property may be absolute, which does not exist outside that species, like 'laughter' for example; for that which is from the five is 'the laugher' not 'laughter' as was said concerning the differentia, and there is no objection to using 'laughter' in place of 'laugher'. And it may not be absolute but relative, which is taken in some things that differ from the species but not in others, so it is a special property for that species relative to what it is not taken in. It is said for substance, for example, that it is not receptive of intensity and weakness, and it is a special property for it not absolutely but relatively. For among accidents there are those like quantity, and what is similar to this has passed before. Then the special property may be coextensive like 'laugher' relative to man, and it may not be but more specific like 'actually laughing', so it necessarily has three divisions in this respect: either it is concomitant like 'potential laugher', or it is not but is non-concomitant like 'actually laughing', and the non-concomitant is either general encompassing every individual of the species like 'actually wondering' or like 'actually angry'. And it may be simple like 'laughter', whether the simple is concomitant or composite; and it may be composite, and the composite may be from special properties of the species or from general ones, or from special and general as was known before. So take heed from what you know.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies al-khassa (the special property), one of the five universals. The author first notes a broader usage that divides special properties into four kinds, but the focus is on the logical definition. The khassa is defined as a universal accident predicated of all individuals of a single species in answer to 'what is it?' — this distinguishes it from genus, species, differentia, and general accident. The author then explains that khassa can be absolute (confined to the species, like 'laugher' for man) or relative (like 'not receptive of intensity' for substance, which is not unique to substance but considered a special property in certain comparisons). Further divisions are given: coextensive vs. more specific (e.g., 'potential laugher' vs. 'actual laugher'), concomitant vs. non-concomitant, and simple vs. composite. Examples are drawn from observable human properties like laughing, wondering, and anger.

Key Terms
الخاصة
كلي عرضي
مقول على الأشخاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which universal is excluded by the phrase 'universal accident' in the definition of khassa?
A) Genus, species, and differentia
B) General accident only
C) Species only
D) Differentia only

Key Takeaway
Al-khassa is defined as a universal accident predicated of all individuals of one species in answer to 'what is it?' — distinguishing it from other universals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the property 'speaking' for man: is it a khassa? Why or why not? Use the definition components.
?
Consider the definition of 'salat' in fiqh: 'specific words and actions, beginning with takbir and ending with taslim'. Identify the genus and differentia. Would khassa apply here?

The Five Universals
beginner
5 min
Absolute and Relative Special Properties
Author distinguishes absolute and relative special properties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute and relative special properties and provide examples of each.

Core Idea
An absolute khassa is confined to one species; a relative khassa is considered special only in comparison to some other category.

The author explains that a special property can be absolute (confined entirely to the species, like 'laugher' for man) or relative (considered special only in certain comparisons, like 'not receptive of intensity' for substance). This distinction is crucial when applying the concept across different sciences.

Guided Path
1
Find the passage on absolute and relative khassa
Look for the sentences starting with 'وإنها أي الخاصة قد تكون مطلقة وهي التي لا توجد خارجة عن ذلك النوع' and ending with 'بل بالنسبة إلى البعض'.
2
Understand absolute khassa
An absolute khassa does not exist outside the species. Example: 'الضاحك' (the laugher) for man. Note the author's distinction between 'الضاحك' (laugher as a capacity) and 'الضحك' (laughter as an act).
The author says the proper khassa in the five universals is 'الضاحك' (potential laugher), not 'الضحك' (actual laughter).
3
Understand relative khassa
A relative khassa is taken in some things that differ from the species but not in others. Example: substance's property 'not receptive of intensity and weakness' is a khassa for substance when compared to quantity, but not when compared to all categories.
The author says it is 'خاصة له لا بالنسبة إلى الكل؛ بل بالنسبة إلى البعض'.
4
Apply the distinction
Given a property, decide if it is unique to a species in all contexts (absolute) or only in specific comparisons (relative).

Concomitant, Non-Concomitant, Simple, and Composite Special Properties
Author divides khassa into concomitant/non-concomitant and simple/composite

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify special properties as concomitant or non-concomitant, simple or composite, and give examples from the text.

Core Idea
Khassa can be always present (concomitant) or sometimes absent (non-concomitant), and can be a single property (simple) or a combination (composite).

The author further divides the special property by its relation to the species: some are always present (like potential laughter in man), others may be absent (like actual laughter). Additionally, a khassa may be a simple single property or a composite of multiple properties. These distinctions refine our understanding of the concept.

Guided Path
1
Locate the divisions of khassa
Find the passage from 'ثم الخاصة قد تكون مساوية...' to the end of the provided excerpt. This covers coextensiveness, concomitance, and composition.
2
Understand coextensive vs. more specific
The author says khassa may be coextensive (مساوية) like 'الضاحك' for man, or more specific (أخص) like 'الضاحك بالفعل' (actually laughing).
This is a division by extent of application within the species.
3
Identify concomitant and non-concomitant
The author then says khassa is either concomitant (لازمة) like 'الضاحك بالقوة' (potential laugher) or non-concomitant (مفارقة) like 'الضاحك بالفعل' (actually laughing). Non-concomitant can be general (like 'المتعجب بالفعل' for man) or more specific (like 'المتعصب بالفعل'?).
The text uses 'نحو المتعصب بالفعل' which might be 'angry'.
4
Differentiate simple and composite
Finally, khassa may be simple (بسيطة) like 'الضحك' (laughter) or composite (مركبة) like 'الصفات الخاصة للنوع والعامة' (combination of special and general properties).
The composite can be from special properties only, general only, or a mixture.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a non-concomitant special property?
A) الضاحك بالقوة (potential laugher)
B) الضاحك بالفعل (actually laughing)
C) الضحك (laughter) as a simple property
D) الصفات الخاصة للنوع (special properties of the species)

Key Takeaway
Special properties can be concomitant (always present) or non-concomitant (sometimes absent), and simple or composite, providing a detailed framework for analyzing universal accidents.

The General Accident: Definition and Distinction
Author defines al-'arad al-'amm, distinguishes it from other universals, and clarifies it is not the accident category.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the general accident among the five universals, apply its definition to examples, and distinguish it from the accident category.

Core Idea
The general accident is a non-essential predicate common to many species, like 'white', not 'whiteness'.

The third of the five universals, al-'arad al-'amm (general accident), is often confused with the accident category of metaphysics. In this passage, the author clarifies its logical meaning: it is a universal that can be predicated of individuals from multiple species in a non-essential way. Through three equivalent definitions and a careful analysis, he distinguishes it from genus, species, differentia, and special property, and warns against mistaking it for the accident opposite substance.

Guided Path
1
Locate the three definitions
The author gives three definitions of al-'arad al-'amm. Write them down and note the common elements: predication, non-essentiality, and multiplicity of species or individuals.
Definitions: (1) predicated on many different in species non-essentially; (2) predicated on two species non-essentially; (3) universal accident predicated on individuals of species.
2
Analyze the third definition's parts
The phrase 'universal accident' (kulli 'aradi) excludes genus, species, and differentia because they are essential. The phrase 'predicated on individuals of species' excludes differentia (which is essential) and special property (which is specific to one species).
Check: 'universal accident' removes jins, naw', fasl; the rest removes fasl and khassa.
3
Resolve the scope objection
A question arises: if it is predicated on individuals of two species, that already implies it is common to two species. The author responds that 'species' in the plural means two or more, consistent with earlier usage. This does not limit the definition.
4
Distinguish the general accident from the accident category
The example of the general accident is 'white' (al-abyad), not 'whiteness' (al-bayad). 'Whiteness' is an accident category that cannot be predicated of a subject as 'it is it' (you cannot say 'Zayd is whiteness'). But 'white' is a predicate (you say 'Zayd is white').
This is the key shift: logical accident vs. metaphysical accident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في العرض العام ] وأما العرض العام فإنه يعرف بتعريفات مختلفة أيضا : مثل ما يقال إنه المقول على كثيرين مختلفين بالنوع قولا غير ذاتي، ومثل ما يقال إنه المحمول على النوعين حملا غير ذاتي، ومثل ما يقال إنه كلي عرضي مقول على الأشخاص من الأنواع، فقولنا كلي عرضي يخرج الجنس والنوع والفصل، وقولنا مقول على الأشخاص من الأنواع يخرج الفصل والخاصة. ولا يقال كيف يصح هذا وقد كان المقول على الأشخاص من النوعين هو العرض العام، فإن لفظ الجمع وهو الأنواع فيما نحن فيه يطلق على النوعين فصاعدا، وقد مر من قبل أنه المقول على كثيرين مختلفين بالنوع في هذا وفي غيره كذلك، والكلام فيه وفي ذلك على السواء. ثم إنه وهو العرض العام في المثال كالأبيض لا كالبياض، وليس هذا العرض هو العرض الذي يناظر الجوهر كما يظنه أكثر الناس، فإن ذلك لا يحمل على موضوعه بأنه هو، وهذا من جملة ما يحمل عليه كالأبيض. فإنك تقول هذا أبيض أي جوهر شيء ذي بياض ليس بعرض يناظر الجوهر؛ بل الذي يناظره هو البياض، وذلك غير هذا العرض. فإن معنى العرض هنا
Chapter on the general accident. As for the general accident, it is also known by various definitions: such as it is said to be the predicated on many different in species in a non-essential predication, and such as it is said to be the predicate on two species in a non-essential predication, and such as it is said to be a universal accident predicated on the individuals of species. So our saying 'universal accident' excludes genus, species, and differentia; and our saying 'predicated on the individuals of species' excludes the differentia and the special property. And it is not said how can this be correct when the predicated on the individuals of two species is the general accident; for the plural term 'species' in our context applies to two species and more, and it has passed before that it is predicated on many different in species in this and in other things similarly, and the discussion in this and that is the same. Then it, and it is the general accident, in example is like 'white' not like 'whiteness'. And this accident is not the accident that opposes substance as most people think, for that is not predicated on its subject as 'it is it', while this is among what is predicated on it like 'white'. For you say 'this is white', meaning a substance that is a thing possessing whiteness, not an accident opposing substance; rather that which opposes it is 'whiteness', and that is different from this accident. For the meaning of 'accident' here.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage defines al-'arad al-'amm (the general accident), another of the five universals. The author gives three definitions: (1) it is predicated of many things differing in species in a non-essential way; (2) it is predicated of two species non-essentially; (3) it is a universal accident predicated of individuals of various species. The author then analyzes the third definition to show that 'universal accident' excludes genus, species, and differentia, while 'predicated of individuals of species' excludes differentia and special property. A potential objection is raised: if it is predicated of individuals of two species, that already implies it is common to two species, so how does that distinguish it from other universals? The author responds that the plural 'species' here means at least two species, and this is consistent with earlier usage. The crucial point follows: the general accident is exemplified by 'white' (al-abyad), not 'whiteness' (al-bayad). The author warns not to confuse this 'accident' (a universal) with the metaphysical accident category (al-'arad al-muqabil li'l-jawhar). The latter, like 'whiteness', is not predicated of its subject as 'it is it' — you cannot say 'Zayd is whiteness' — whereas the general accident is predicated like 'Zayd is white'. This clarifies the logical role of 'arad al-'amm as a predicate, not a substance.

Critique of Definitions of 'Aradi (Accidental Universal)
Critique of three definitions of 'aradi and explanation of its types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three flawed definitions of 'aradi and explain why each fails according to the author's critique.

Core Idea
Not every accident is separable; some accidents are necessary.

The accidental universal ('aradi) is a key concept in the five universals, often misunderstood. The author critically examines three popular definitions and reveals their logical flaws. This lesson will sharpen your ability to evaluate definitions and avoid common mistakes in classifying predicates.

Guided Path
1
Extract the three definitions
Find the three instances of 'مثل ما يقال' in the Arabic. Each introduces a definition of 'aradi.
2
Analyze the author's objection to each definition
For each definition, note the specific flaw the author points out. Is it a confusion between existence and imagination? A failure to account for perpetual accidents? A mistaking of 'arad (accident) for 'aradi (accidental)?
3
Distinguish necessary from separable accidents
The author explains that 'aradi can be necessary (lazim) or separable (mufariq). Understand that necessary accidents are always present with the subject, while separable ones may be fast or slow to change.
4
Apply the critique to a new example
Test a proposed definition of 'aradi (e.g., 'the accidental is what is not essential') and see if it suffers from any of the same flaws.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العرضي، وإنه بإزاء الذاتي والجوهري بخلاف ذلك وهو العرض، فإنه بإزاء الذات والجوهر؛ والذاتي قد يكون جوهرًا وقد يكون عرضًا، فكذلك العرضي قد يكون جوهرًا وقد يكون عرضًا.
وإنه يعرف بتعريفات فاسدة مثل ما يقال: إنه هو الذي يكون ويفسد من غير فساد الموضوع، وفيه من الفساد؛ فإنه إن عُني بالكون والفساد حال ما يكون ويفسد في الوجود فالعرض العام اللازم ليس كذلك، وإن عُني به ما يكون في الوجود والوهم جميعًا فكثيرا ما تكون الأمور العرضية غير الذاتية ما إذا رفع بالتوهم استحال أن يبقى ذلك الشيء كما كان.
ومثل ما يقال هو الذي يمكن أن يوجد لشيء واحد بعينه، وأن لا يوجد، وفيه من الفساد أيضًا؛ فإن كثيرا من الأعراض ما يكون دائما، والدائم لا يمكن أن لا يوجد إلا في الوهم.
ومثل ما يقال إنه الذي ليس بجنس ولا فصل ولا نوع ولا خاصة وهو أبدا قائم في موضوع، وفيه من الفساد ما فيه أيضًا؛ فإن كل شخص من الأعراض يشارك فيه؛ إذ الطبائع من حيث هي طبائع لا من حيث هي كلية. فإن الحق به أنه كلي بهذه الصفة يكون مطابقا، لكن قوله «إنه قائم في موضوع على ظن أن ما يكون من الخمسة هو العرض الذي يناظر الجوهر»، فذلك باطل لما مر من قبل أن المعنى من العرض هنا هو العرضي.
وإنه قد يكون عاما للجنس كالوجود وقد يكون عاما للنوع دون الجنس كالمتنفس فإنه عرض عام للإنسان وخاصة للحيوان. وكذلك قد يكون لازمًا كالموجود بالقوة وقد لا يكون بل يكون مفارقًا. وذلك قد يكون عاماً يعم جميع الأفراد كالمتنفس وقد لا يكون فيكون على ثلاثة أقسام على مثال الخاصة؛ إذ لا يكون لازما من الخاصة والعرض العام، فذلك قد يكون مفارقًا سريع الزوال كحمرة الخجل وصفرة الوجل وقد يكون مفارقاً بطيء الزوال كالشيب والشباب. فخاصة الخاص كالضاحك للإنسان مثلاً خاصة العام وهو الحيوان وجنسه وجنس جنسه بدون عكس؛ فإن ما يكون خاصة للعام كالنفس للحيوان مثلا فلا يلزم أن يكون خاصة الخاص وعرض عام العام نحو المتحرك للحيوان عرض عام الخاص وهو الإنسان مثلا بدون عكس، فإن عرض العام الخاص كالمتنفس لا يلزم أن يكون عرضًا عاما العام. وكيف وهو خاصة العام في هذه الصورة كما مر ؟
The accidental (al-'aradi) is opposite to the essential and the substantial; and the accident (al-'arad) is opposite to the essence and substance. The essential may be substance and may be accident, similarly the accidental may be substance and may be accident. It is defined by corrupt definitions such as it is said: it is that which comes to be and passes away without the corruption of the subject, and there is corruption in it; for if by coming-to-be and passing away is meant the state of its coming-to-be and passing away in existence, then the universal necessary accident is not like that; and if by it is meant what is in existence and imagination together, then often the accidental non-essential matters are such that if they are removed by imagination, it is impossible for that thing to remain as it was. And like what is said: it is that which can exist for one particular thing and not exist, and there is corruption in it as well; for many accidents are perpetual, and the perpetual cannot not exist except in imagination. And like what is said: it is that which is not a genus nor a species nor a differentia nor a property, and it always subsists in a subject, and there is corruption in it as well; for every individual of accidents shares in it; since natures as such are not universal. For the truth about it is that it is a universal of this description that corresponds, but his saying 'it subsists in a subject' based on the assumption that what is among the five is the accident that corresponds to substance, that is false for the reason that the meaning of 'accident' here is 'accidental'. And it may be universal for the genus like existence, and may be universal for the species without the genus like 'respirant' for it is a universal accident for man and a property for animal. And similarly it may be necessary like 'existent in potentiality' and may not be but rather be separable. And that may be universal encompassing all individuals like 'respirant' and may not be, so it is in three divisions like property; for it is not necessary from property and universal accident, that may be quickly separable like the redness of shyness and the yellowness of fear, and may be slowly separable like greying and youth. So the property of the property like 'risible' for man for example, the property of the universal which is animal and its genus and the genus of its genus without converse; for what is property for the universal like 'soul' for animal for example does not necessarily become property of the property, and the universal accident of the universal like 'mobile' for animal is a universal accident of the property which is man for example without converse, for the accident of the universal property like 'respirant' does not necessarily become a universal accident of the universal. And how, given that it is a property of the universal in this form as passed?
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage critically examines several flawed definitions of the term 'aradi (accidental universal) and clarifies its true nature. The author begins by positioning 'aradi as the opposite of dhati (essential) and jawhari (substantial), noting that both essential and accidental can be either substance or accident. He then presents three common but corrupt definitions. The first definition claims that the accidental is what comes to be and passes away without the subject's corruption. The author objects: if 'coming to be and passing away' refers to actual existence, then necessary universal accidents do not fit; if it refers to both existence and imagination, then many accidental non-essential matters cannot be removed by imagination without destroying the subject. The second definition says the accidental can either exist or not exist for a particular thing. The author counters that many accidents are perpetual, and what is perpetual cannot not exist except in imagination. The third definition describes the accidental as not being a genus, differentia, species, or property, and always subsisting in a subject. The author replies that every individual accident shares in that characteristic, and the claim 'subsists in a subject' mistakenly identifies 'arad (accident) with the category accident, whereas here the intended meaning is 'aradi (accidental universal). The author then elaborates on the nature of 'aradi: it can be universal for the genus (e.g., existence) or universal for the species without the genus (e.g., 'respirant' for man). It can be necessary (e.g., existent in potentiality) or separable. Separable accidents may be quickly passing (e.g., blushing) or slow (e.g., grey hair). The discussion continues with examples distinguishing property (khassa) and universal accident, emphasizing that properties of a universal do not necessarily become properties of the property, and accidents of a universal do not necessarily become accidents of the universal. Throughout, the author uses examples from common experience to illustrate logical distinctions.

الذاتي
العرض العام اللازم
المفارق
