Commonalities Among All Five Universals
Author identifies the two properties shared by all five universals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two properties shared by all five universals (universality and relativity) and explain why each universal is relative to its particulars.

Core Idea
All five universals are one in being universal and relative – but they differ in how they answer 'what is it?' versus 'which thing is it?'

In this passage, the author begins by stating that for the universal to be divided into five divisions, each division must itself be universal. Moreover, each universal is from the category of relation (maqūla al-muḍāf), meaning it is always relative to what is particular under it. This lesson unpacks these two fundamental commonalities that bind all five universals together.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the grouping
The author discusses the five universals together. Identify what all five share: they are all universals and they are all relative. These are the two properties common to every one of the five.
The Arabic term for commonality is mushtarakāt.
2
Understand universality
Each of the five is a universal, meaning it can be predicated of many things. The author says this is necessary because the universal itself is divided into these five, so each must be a universal.
Universality is the primary shared characteristic.
3
Understand relativity
Each universal is from the category of relation. That means it exists only in relation to something else – the particulars that fall under it. Without those particulars, the universal would not be a universal.
The text says 'else it would not be particular relative to it'.
4
Test with an example from fiqh
Take the universal 'forbidden (ḥarām)'. Why is it universal? It applies to many actions. Why is it relative? Because it is always about specific acts that are forbidden (e.g., drinking khamr). If there were no particular forbidden acts, the category 'forbidden' would have no meaning.
This example shows how universality and relativity are inseparable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في المشاركات والمباينات والمناسبات بين هذه الخمسة ]
أما الكلي فإنه إذا كان منقسما إلى الأقسام الخمسة التي [مر] ذكرها كان من اللوازم أن تكون الأقسام مشتملة على المشاركات والمباينات والمناسبات. والاطلاع على كل واحد من الأقسام وإن كان متضمناً لشأنها فالتعرض لذلك على التفصيل لا يكون خاليًا عن الفائدة؛ بل يكون مشتملا عليها.
أما «المشاركات»، الكلي لا يكون منقسمًا إلى هذه الأقسام إلا وأن يكون كل واحد منها كلياً فتكون الكلية هي المشترك فيها بالضرورة، والكلي محمول بالطبع على ما يكون جزئياً بالنسبة فكذلك كل واحد من الأقسام وإنه من مقولة المضاف لا محالة وإلا لا يكون غيره جزئيًا بالنسبة إليه. فكل واحد من الأقسام كذلك وإنها هي الخماسيات لا يشتمل كل واحد من الخمسة عليها. وأما الأربعة منها وهي الجنس والفصل / والنوع والخاصة فمن المشترك [۳۲] بينها أنه لا يمكن اشتراك جميع الموجودات في واحد منها. وإن كل واحد منها يخالف العرض العام بذاته وإنه قسم لذلك وإنها هي الرباعيات. وأما الثلاثة منها وهي الجنس والفصل والنوع، فإنها من الأمور الذاتية، وإنها مقولة بالنسبة على ما تحتها ومخالفة لما هو الخارج عن الذات على الخاصة والعرض العام، وإنها هي الثلاثيات. وأما الاثنان منها وهما الجنس والفصل فإنهما جزءا الماهية فيشتركان فيما هو من لوازم الجزئية. وأما إذا كان على العكس فذلك في الخماسيات ظاهر؛ إذ المشاركات بينها هي التي مرَّ ذكرها من قبل. وأما في الرباعيات فلأن كل واحد منها قسم للجنس ومخالفًا له وممتنعا بدونه، وفي الثلاثيات أنه لا يمكن أن يكون واحد منها بدون النوع وأن يكون منها بنفسه أبدا، وأن لا يكون قسما لما عداها من الخمسة؛ وفي الثنائيات أن يكون كل واحد منها زائدا على النوع وقائمًا به وقسمًا لما عداها من الخمسة.
وأما المشاركات الباقية بين كل واحد منها وحده وبين غيره فإنها متعددة لا بد للتعرض لما هو من المشهورات منها.
أما الجنس والفصل فقد مرَّ الكلام فيهما كلاما جامعا يشير إلى كثير من المشاركات، ولأنهما يشتركان في أن لا يمتنع في طبيعة كل واحد منهما أن يحمل على النوع الواحد وغيره، فقد مرَّ الكلام فيه ويشتركان أيضًا في أن كل ما يحمل عليهما من طريق ما هو فذلك يحمل على ما تحتهما من طريق ما هو، ويُعنى بهذا الحمل غير ما يُعنى به في جواب ما هو . وكذلك في أن رفعهما هو رفع ما تحتهما من الأنواع فإذا رفعت الحيوانية والنطق رفع الإنسان لا محالة. وأما الجنس والنوع فإنهما يشتركان في كون كل واحد منهما مقولاً في جواب ما هو . وكذلك في أنهما يتقدمان على ما يحملان عليه وهو الذي له جنس ونوع، وفي أن كل واحد منهما ماهية كل واحد من جزئياته بالشركة. وأما الجنس والخاصة فإنهما يشتركان في أن الرسم الكامل لا يتركب إلا منهما فيكونا جزئية، ولأنهما يحملان على النوع دائمًا ويتبعانه كذلك. وأما الجنس مع العرض العام فإنهما يشتركان في كون كل واحد منهما مقولا على المختلفة بالحقائق ومفتقرا في الوجود إليها.
وأما الفصل والنوع فإنهما يشتركان في كون كل واحد منهما مقولاً على المختلفة بالعدد فقط، ومحمولا على ما تحتهما بالسوية؛ وأما الفصل والخاصة فإنهما يشتركان في اختصاصهما بالنوع وفي كل واحد منهما مقولاً في جواب أي شيء ما هو؛ وأما الفصل والعرض العام فإنهما يشتركان في أنه يمكن أن يعم كل واحد منهما هذا النوع وغيره من الأنواع وفي أنهما على الدوام لموضوعاتهما؛ وهذا بالنسبة إلى غير المفارق.
Section: On the commonalities, differences, and relationships among these five.
As for the universal, if it is divided into the five divisions that were mentioned, it follows that the divisions include commonalities, differences, and relationships. Becoming acquainted with each of the divisions—even if it includes their own characteristics—yet treating that in detail is not devoid of benefit; rather it includes benefit.
As for the 'commonalities': the universal is not divided into these divisions unless each of them is universal, so universality is what is shared among them necessarily. The universal is predicated naturally on what is particular relative to it, and likewise each of the divisions. And it is from the category of the relative without doubt, otherwise what is other than it would not be particular relative to it. So each of the divisions is like that. And the fives are such that not every one of the five is included in them. As for the four of them, namely genus, differentia, species, and property, what is common among them is that it is impossible for all existents to share in any one of them. And that each of them differs from general accident in itself, and it is a division of it. And that is the fours. As for the three of them, namely genus, differentia, and species, they are essential matters. And they are predicated relatively on what is under them, and they differ from what is external to the essence, such as property and general accident. And that is the threes. As for the two of them, namely genus and differentia, they are parts of the quiddity, so they share in what is among the concomitants of part-hood. As for when it is the contrary, that is apparent in the fives, since the commonalities among them are those mentioned before. And as for the fours, because each of them is a division of genus, different from it, and impossible without it. And in the threes, that none of them can be without the species, and that it is, by itself, never a division of what is other than them from the five. And in the twos, that each of them is additional to the species, subsisting by it, and a division of what is other than them from the five.
As for the remaining commonalities between each one alone and its other, they are numerous, and it is necessary to address what is well-known among them.
As for genus and differentia, the discussion of them has passed in a comprehensive way indicating many commonalities. They share in that it is not impossible for the nature of each of them to be predicated on one species and other than it. The discussion of that has passed. And they also share in that everything predicated on them by way of 'what it is' is predicated on what is under them by way of 'what it is', and what is meant by this predication is other than what is meant in the answer to 'what it is'. Likewise, raising them is raising what is under them of species. So if animality and rationality are raised, then humanity is necessarily raised. As for genus and species, they share in that each of them is said in answer to 'what it is'. And also that they are prior to what they are predicated on, which is what has genus and species, and that each of them is the quiddity of each of its particulars by sharing. As for genus and property, they share in that the complete description (rasm) is composed only of them, so they are partial, and because they are always predicated on the species and follow it likewise. As for genus with general accident, they share in that each of them is said of things differing in realities and is in need, in existence, of them.
As for differentia and species, they share in that each of them is said of things differing only in number, and is predicated equally on what is under them. As for differentia and property, they share in their being specific to the species and that each of them is said in answer to 'which thing is it?'. As for differentia and general accident, they share in that it is possible for each of them to include this species and other species, and that they are constantly attached to their subjects. This is with respect to inseparable accidents.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the commonalities (mushtarakāt) among the five universals (al-kulliyāt al-khams): genus (jins), differentia (fasl), species (naw‘), property (khāṣṣa), and general accident (‘araḍ ‘āmm). The author begins by stating that since the universal is divided into these five, it is necessary to examine what they share, how they differ, and their interrelations. The discussion proceeds in a hierarchical manner, first grouping the five into categories based on shared properties.

The first grouping is all five (al-khamīsiyyāt): each is universal and thus shares universality. Each is also relative (min maqūla al-muḍāf) because what is other than it is particular relative to it.

The second grouping is four (al-rubā‘iyyāt): genus, differentia, species, and property. They share that no single one of them can encompass all existents, and each differs in essence from general accident.

The third grouping is three (al-thulāthiyyāt): genus, differentia, and species. They are essential (dhātiyya) and are predicated relatively on what is under them, differing from non-essentials like property and general accident.

The fourth grouping is two (al-thunā’iyyāt): genus and differentia, which are parts of the quiddity (māhiyya) and share the property of being parts.

Then the author discusses specific pairs in detail, explaining their shared characteristics using standard logical examples. For instance, genus and differentia share that raising them (i.e., removing them) raises the species—using the example that if animality and rationality are raised, humanity is necessarily raised. Genus and species both answer 'what is it?' and constitute the quiddity of their particulars by sharing. Genus and property together form the complete description (rasm). Genus and general accident both are said of things differing in realities and depend on those things for existence. Differentia and species both are said of things differing in number and predicated equally. Differentia and property are specific to the species and answer 'which thing is it?'. Differentia and general accident can encompass multiple species and are constantly attached.

The passage is carefully structured, moving from broader groupings to specific pairs, and each claim is justified with logical reasoning. The author assumes familiarity with the standard definitions of the five universals and their roles in definition and predication.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what two properties are shared by all five universals?
A) Essentiality and non-essentiality
B) Universality and being from the category of relation
C) Being part of the quiddity and being predicated equally
D) Answering 'what is it?' and 'which thing is it?'

Key Takeaway
All five universals are necessarily universal and relative – these two properties are the foundation for all deeper commonalities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the term 'salat' (prayer). Is it a universal? If so, what are its particulars? Explain how it exhibits both universality and relativity.
?
Choose any one of the five universals (e.g., species). Write one sentence explaining why it is universal and one sentence explaining why it is relative, using an Islamic example (e.g., the species 'human' for Zayd and 'Amr).

The Five Universals
intermediate
7 min
What Four Universals Share: Genus, Differentia, Species, and Property
Author states two commonalities for the four universals excluding general accident

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify two properties shared by genus, differentia, species, and property but not by general accident: impossibility of encompassing all existents and essential difference from general accident.

Core Idea
Four universals share that they cannot be predicated of all existents and are essentially different from general accident.

After discussing the commonalities of all five, the author narrows his focus to four universals: genus, differentia, species, and property. He points out two things they share: first, none of them can be truly predicated of every existent thing; second, each of them differs in its very essence (dhāt) from general accident. This lesson examines these shared characteristics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four universals
The passage lists the four: genus (jins), differentia (fasl), species (naw‘), and property (khāṣṣa). The fifth universal, general accident (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm), is excluded.
Note that these four are sometimes called the 'four' (rubāʿiyyāt).
2
Understand the first commonality: not all existents
No single universal among these four can be truly said of every being. For example, 'animal' (genus) does not apply to rocks; 'rational' (differentia) does not apply to horses; 'human' (species) does not apply to angels; 'laughing' (property) does not apply to all humans at all times (only potentially).
General accident, however, can be said of many things across species, e.g., 'moving' applies to animals and inanimate objects.
3
Understand the second commonality: essential difference from general accident
Each of the four differs in its very essence (bi-dhātihi) from general accident. This means that their definitions are fundamentally distinct from the definition of general accident. For instance, genus is essential, general accident is not.
The text says 'each of them differs from general accident in itself'.
4
Apply to a fiqhi example
Take the universal 'permissible (ḥalāl)'. This is a general accident (ʿaraḍ ʿāmm) because it applies to many different types of actions across species. In contrast, a property like 'illicit (ḥarām)' is specific to certain actions and cannot be said of all existents. Compare: 'ḥalāl' can be said of food, drink, acts of worship, etc., but 'ḥarām' is restricted.
This illustrates why general accident is excluded from the four.

The Five Universals
intermediate
7 min
The Three Essential Universals: Genus, Differentia, Species
Author states that genus, differentia, and species are essential and relatively predicated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three essential universals and state the two properties they share: being essential (dhātiyya) and being predicated relatively on what is under them.

Core Idea
Genus, differentia, and species are essential – they answer 'what is it?' and are predicated relatively, unlike property and general accident.

The author now examines the three universals that are essential (dhātiyya): genus, differentia, and species. He notes two commonalities among them: they are essential matters, and they are predicated relatively on what is under them. This means they express something about the essence of the things they are said of, and they are always considered in relation to the species or individuals that fall under them. In contrast, property and general accident are outside the essence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three essential universals
The passage names genus, differentia, and species as the essential ones. They are called the 'threes' (thulāthiyyāt).
Check the definitions: genus answers 'what is it?' broadly; differentia answers 'which thing is it?'; species answers 'what is it?' specifically.
2
Understand 'essential matters'
These three are dhātiyya, meaning they constitute or express the essence of a thing. For example, for a human, 'animal' (genus) and 'rational' (differentia) together form the essence; 'human' (species) is the essence itself.
Property and general accident are non-essential (ʿaraḍiyya).
3
Understand relative predication
Each of these three is predicated relatively on what is under it. The genus is said of species, the differentia is said of individuals, the species is said of individuals – but always in relation. For instance, 'animal' is said of 'human' and 'horse'; 'rational' is said of 'human'; 'human' is said of 'Zayd'.
The text says 'they are predicated relatively on what is under them'.
4
Contrast with non-essentials
Property and general accident are not essential. The text explicitly says these three 'differ from what is external to the essence, such as property and general accident'. For example, 'laughing' (property) is not essential to being human; 'walking' (general accident) is not essential.
This distinction is crucial for sound definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في المشاركات والمباينات والمناسبات بين هذه الخمسة ]
أما الكلي فإنه إذا كان منقسما إلى الأقسام الخمسة التي [مر] ذكرها كان من اللوازم أن تكون الأقسام مشتملة على المشاركات والمباينات والمناسبات. والاطلاع على كل واحد من الأقسام وإن كان متضمناً لشأنها فالتعرض لذلك على التفصيل لا يكون خاليًا عن الفائدة؛ بل يكون مشتملا عليها.
أما «المشاركات»، الكلي لا يكون منقسمًا إلى هذه الأقسام إلا وأن يكون كل واحد منها كلياً فتكون الكلية هي المشترك فيها بالضرورة، والكلي محمول بالطبع على ما يكون جزئياً بالنسبة فكذلك كل واحد من الأقسام وإنه من مقولة المضاف لا محالة وإلا لا يكون غيره جزئيًا بالنسبة إليه. فكل واحد من الأقسام كذلك وإنها هي الخماسيات لا يشتمل كل واحد من الخمسة عليها. وأما الأربعة منها وهي الجنس والفصل / والنوع والخاصة فمن المشترك [۳۲] بينها أنه لا يمكن اشتراك جميع الموجودات في واحد منها. وإن كل واحد منها يخالف العرض العام بذاته وإنه قسم لذلك وإنها هي الرباعيات. وأما الثلاثة منها وهي الجنس والفصل والنوع، فإنها من الأمور الذاتية، وإنها مقولة بالنسبة على ما تحتها ومخالفة لما هو الخارج عن الذات على الخاصة والعرض العام، وإنها هي الثلاثيات. وأما الاثنان منها وهما الجنس والفصل فإنهما جزءا الماهية فيشتركان فيما هو من لوازم الجزئية. وأما إذا كان على العكس فذلك في الخماسيات ظاهر؛ إذ المشاركات بينها هي التي مرَّ ذكرها من قبل. وأما في الرباعيات فلأن كل واحد منها قسم للجنس ومخالفًا له وممتنعا بدونه، وفي الثلاثيات أنه لا يمكن أن يكون واحد منها بدون النوع وأن يكون منها بنفسه أبدا، وأن لا يكون قسما لما عداها من الخمسة؛ وفي الثنائيات أن يكون كل واحد منها زائدا على النوع وقائمًا به وقسمًا لما عداها من الخمسة.
وأما المشاركات الباقية بين كل واحد منها وحده وبين غيره فإنها متعددة لا بد للتعرض لما هو من المشهورات منها.
أما الجنس والفصل فقد مرَّ الكلام فيهما كلاما جامعا يشير إلى كثير من المشاركات، ولأنهما يشتركان في أن لا يمتنع في طبيعة كل واحد منهما أن يحمل على النوع الواحد وغيره، فقد مرَّ الكلام فيه ويشتركان أيضًا في أن كل ما يحمل عليهما من طريق ما هو فذلك يحمل على ما تحتهما من طريق ما هو، ويُعنى بهذا الحمل غير ما يُعنى به في جواب ما هو . وكذلك في أن رفعهما هو رفع ما تحتهما من الأنواع فإذا رفعت الحيوانية والنطق رفع الإنسان لا محالة. وأما الجنس والنوع فإنهما يشتركان في كون كل واحد منهما مقولاً في جواب ما هو . وكذلك في أنهما يتقدمان على ما يحملان عليه وهو الذي له جنس ونوع، وفي أن كل واحد منهما ماهية كل واحد من جزئياته بالشركة. وأما الجنس والخاصة فإنهما يشتركان في أن الرسم الكامل لا يتركب إلا منهما فيكونا جزئية، ولأنهما يحملان على النوع دائمًا ويتبعانه كذلك. وأما الجنس مع العرض العام فإنهما يشتركان في كون كل واحد منهما مقولا على المختلفة بالحقائق ومفتقرا في الوجود إليها.
وأما الفصل والنوع فإنهما يشتركان في كون كل واحد منهما مقولاً على المختلفة بالعدد فقط، ومحمولا على ما تحتهما بالسوية؛ وأما الفصل والخاصة فإنهما يشتركان في اختصاصهما بالنوع وفي كل واحد منهما مقولاً في جواب أي شيء ما هو؛ وأما الفصل والعرض العام فإنهما يشتركان في أنه يمكن أن يعم كل واحد منهما هذا النوع وغيره من الأنواع وفي أنهما على الدوام لموضوعاتهما؛ وهذا بالنسبة إلى غير المفارق.
Section: On the commonalities, differences, and relationships among these five.
As for the universal, if it is divided into the five divisions that were mentioned, it follows that the divisions include commonalities, differences, and relationships. Becoming acquainted with each of the divisions—even if it includes their own characteristics—yet treating that in detail is not devoid of benefit; rather it includes benefit.
As for the 'commonalities': the universal is not divided into these divisions unless each of them is universal, so universality is what is shared among them necessarily. The universal is predicated naturally on what is particular relative to it, and likewise each of the divisions. And it is from the category of the relative without doubt, otherwise what is other than it would not be particular relative to it. So each of the divisions is like that. And the fives are such that not every one of the five is included in them. As for the four of them, namely genus, differentia, species, and property, what is common among them is that it is impossible for all existents to share in any one of them. And that each of them differs from general accident in itself, and it is a division of it. And that is the fours. As for the three of them, namely genus, differentia, and species, they are essential matters. And they are predicated relatively on what is under them, and they differ from what is external to the essence, such as property and general accident. And that is the threes. As for the two of them, namely genus and differentia, they are parts of the quiddity, so they share in what is among the concomitants of part-hood. As for when it is the contrary, that is apparent in the fives, since the commonalities among them are those mentioned before. And as for the fours, because each of them is a division of genus, different from it, and impossible without it. And in the threes, that none of them can be without the species, and that it is, by itself, never a division of what is other than them from the five. And in the twos, that each of them is additional to the species, subsisting by it, and a division of what is other than them from the five.
As for the remaining commonalities between each one alone and its other, they are numerous, and it is necessary to address what is well-known among them.
As for genus and differentia, the discussion of them has passed in a comprehensive way indicating many commonalities. They share in that it is not impossible for the nature of each of them to be predicated on one species and other than it. The discussion of that has passed. And they also share in that everything predicated on them by way of 'what it is' is predicated on what is under them by way of 'what it is', and what is meant by this predication is other than what is meant in the answer to 'what it is'. Likewise, raising them is raising what is under them of species. So if animality and rationality are raised, then humanity is necessarily raised. As for genus and species, they share in that each of them is said in answer to 'what it is'. And also that they are prior to what they are predicated on, which is what has genus and species, and that each of them is the quiddity of each of its particulars by sharing. As for genus and property, they share in that the complete description (rasm) is composed only of them, so they are partial, and because they are always predicated on the species and follow it likewise. As for genus with general accident, they share in that each of them is said of things differing in realities and is in need, in existence, of them.
As for differentia and species, they share in that each of them is said of things differing only in number, and is predicated equally on what is under them. As for differentia and property, they share in their being specific to the species and that each of them is said in answer to 'which thing is it?'. As for differentia and general accident, they share in that it is possible for each of them to include this species and other species, and that they are constantly attached to their subjects. This is with respect to inseparable accidents.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the commonalities (mushtarakāt) among the five universals (al-kulliyāt al-khams): genus (jins), differentia (fasl), species (naw‘), property (khāṣṣa), and general accident (‘araḍ ‘āmm). The author begins by stating that since the universal is divided into these five, it is necessary to examine what they share, how they differ, and their interrelations. The discussion proceeds in a hierarchical manner, first grouping the five into categories based on shared properties.

The first grouping is all five (al-khamīsiyyāt): each is universal and thus shares universality. Each is also relative (min maqūla al-muḍāf) because what is other than it is particular relative to it.

The second grouping is four (al-rubā‘iyyāt): genus, differentia, species, and property. They share that no single one of them can encompass all existents, and each differs in essence from general accident.

The third grouping is three (al-thulāthiyyāt): genus, differentia, and species. They are essential (dhātiyya) and are predicated relatively on what is under them, differing from non-essentials like property and general accident.

The fourth grouping is two (al-thunā’iyyāt): genus and differentia, which are parts of the quiddity (māhiyya) and share the property of being parts.

Then the author discusses specific pairs in detail, explaining their shared characteristics using standard logical examples. For instance, genus and differentia share that raising them (i.e., removing them) raises the species—using the example that if animality and rationality are raised, humanity is necessarily raised. Genus and species both answer 'what is it?' and constitute the quiddity of their particulars by sharing. Genus and property together form the complete description (rasm). Genus and general accident both are said of things differing in realities and depend on those things for existence. Differentia and species both are said of things differing in number and predicated equally. Differentia and property are specific to the species and answer 'which thing is it?'. Differentia and general accident can encompass multiple species and are constantly attached.

The passage is carefully structured, moving from broader groupings to specific pairs, and each claim is justified with logical reasoning. The author assumes familiarity with the standard definitions of the five universals and their roles in definition and predication.

Key Takeaway
Genus, differentia, and species are essential and are always predicated relatively – they form the core of any real definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Consider the term 'ḥajj' (pilgrimage). Identify its genus (e.g., 'worship act') and differentia (e.g., 'performed at Makkah at a specific time'). Is 'ḥajj' an essential universal? Why?

The Five Universals

6 min
Genus and Differentia: Parts of the Quiddity
Author states that genus and differentia are parts of the quiddity and share in the concomitants of part-hood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why genus and differentia are called parts of the quiddity and state that they share the property of being part of the essence.

Core Idea
Genus and differentia are the two parts of the quiddity – they share the status of being essential components.

After discussing the fives, fours, and threes, the author focuses on the pair of genus and differentia. He states that they are the parts of the quiddity (māhiyya) and thus share in the concomitants of part-hood. This means that just as parts of a whole share certain characteristics, genus and differentia share being components of the definition. This lesson delves into that relationship.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the pair
The passage names genus and differentia as the two that are parts of the quiddity. They are called the 'twos' (thunā’iyyāt).
Quiddity (māhiyya) means essence or 'whatness'.
2
Understand 'parts of the quiddity'
Genus and differentia together constitute the essence of a species. For example, 'animal' (genus) and 'rational' (differentia) together give the essence of 'human'. The species itself is not a part but the whole composed of genus and differentia.
Species is the composite, not a part.
3
Identify the shared property: concomitants of part-hood
The author says they share in what is among the concomitants (lawāzim) of part-hood. This means things that follow from being a part: for example, parts cannot subsist without the whole, they are less comprehensive than the whole, etc.
The text does not list these concomitants explicitly, but they are implied.
4
Apply to a definition in fiqh
Take the definition of 'muʿāmala' (financial transaction). Its genus might be 'contract' and its differentia might be 'exchange of property'. Both are parts of the quiddity of muʿāmala. They share that they are essential components without which the definition would be incomplete.
This shows how genus and differentia function together in defining terms.

Section: On the commonalities, differences, and relationships among these five.
As for the universal, if it is divided into the five divisions that were mentioned, it follows that the divisions include commonalities, differences, and relationships. Becoming acquainted with each of the divisions—even if it includes their own characteristics—yet treating that in detail is not devoid of benefit; rather it includes benefit.

The universal is predicated naturally on what is particular relative to it, and likewise each of the divisions. And it is from the category of the relative without doubt, otherwise what is other than it would not be particular relative to it. So each of the divisions is like that. And the fives are such that not every one of the five is included in them. As for the four of them, namely genus, differentia, species, and property, what is common among them is that it is impossible for all existents to share in any one of them. And that each of them differs from general accident in itself, and it is a division of it. And that is the fours. As for the three of them, namely genus, differentia, and species, they are essential matters. And they are predicated relatively on what is under them, and they differ from what is external to the essence, such as property and general accident. And that is the threes. As for the two of them, namely genus and differentia, they are parts of the quiddity, so they share in what is among the concomitants of part-hood. As for when it is the contrary, that is apparent in the fives, since the commonalities among them are those mentioned before. And as for the fours, because each of them is a division of genus, different from it, and impossible without it. And in the threes, that none of them can be without the species, and that it is, by itself, never a division of what is other than them from the five. And in the twos, that each of them is additional to the species, subsisting by it, and a division of what is other than them from the five.
As for the remaining commonalities between each one alone and its other, they are numerous, and it is necessary to address what is well-known among them.
As for genus and differentia, the discussion of them has passed in a comprehensive way indicating many commonalities. They share in that it is not impossible for the nature of each of them to be predicated on one species and other than it. The discussion of that has passed. And they also share in that everything predicated on them by way of 'what it is' is predicated on what is under them by way of 'what it is', and what is meant by this predication is other than what is meant in the answer to 'what it is'. As for genus and species, they share in that each of them is said in answer to 'what it is'. And also that they are prior to what they are predicated on, which is what has genus and species, and that each of them is the quiddity of each of its particulars by sharing. As for genus and property, they share in that the complete description (rasm) is composed only of them, so they are partial, and because they are always predicated on the species and follow it likewise. As for genus with general accident, they share in that each of them is said of things differing in realities and is in need, in existence, of them.
As for differentia and species, they share in that each of them is said of things differing only in number, and is predicated equally on what is under them. As for differentia and property, they share in their being specific to the species and that each of them is said in answer to 'which thing is it?'. As for differentia and general accident, they share in that it is possible for each of them to include this species and other species, and that they are constantly attached to their subjects. This is with respect to inseparable accidents.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the commonalities (mushtarakāt) among the five universals (al-kulliyāt al-khams): genus (jins), differentia (fasl), species (naw‘), property (khāṣṣa), and general accident (‘araḍ ‘āmm). The author begins by stating that since the universal is divided into these five, it is necessary to examine what they share, how they differ, and their interrelations. The discussion proceeds in a hierarchical manner, first grouping the five into categories based on shared properties.

The first grouping is all five (al-khamīsiyyāt): each is universal and thus shares universality. Each is also relative (min maqūla al-muḍāf) because what is other than it is particular relative to it.

The second grouping is four (al-rubā‘iyyāt): genus, differentia, species, and property. They share that no single one of them can encompass all existents, and each differs in essence from general accident.

The third grouping is three (al-thulāthiyyāt): genus, differentia, and species. They are essential (dhātiyya) and are predicated relatively on what is under them, differing from non-essentials like property and general accident.

The fourth grouping is two (al-thunā’iyyāt): genus and differentia, which are parts of the quiddity (māhiyya) and share the property of being parts.

Then the author discusses specific pairs in detail, explaining their shared characteristics using standard logical examples. For instance, genus and differentia share that raising them (i.e., removing them) raises the species—using the example that if animality and rationality are raised, humanity is necessarily raised. Genus and species both answer 'what is it?' and constitute the quiddity of their particulars by sharing. Genus and property together form the complete description (rasm). Genus and general accident both are said of things differing in realities and depend on those things for existence. Differentia and species both are said of things differing in number and predicated equally. Differentia and property are specific to the species and answer 'which thing is it?'. Differentia and general accident can encompass multiple species and are constantly attached.

The passage is carefully structured, moving from broader groupings to specific pairs, and each claim is justified with logical reasoning. The author assumes familiarity with the standard definitions of the five universals and their roles in definition and predication.

الجنس

Key Takeaway
Genus and differentia are the two parts of the quiddity – their commonality is being essential components that together form the species.

Commonalities Involving Genus and Differentia
Author enumerates shared characteristics between each pair of the five universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the shared logical properties of genus and differentia and distinguish them from commonalities with other universals.

Core Idea
Genus and differentia share that their removal removes the species.

This passage details the commonalities (mushtarakat) among the five universals. We will focus on the pairs involving genus (jins) and differentia (fasl), examining their unique logical properties and how they compare with other pairs. Understanding these relationships is crucial for precise definition in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Locate genus-and-differentia pairs
In the Arabic text, find the section that begins with 'وأما الجنس والفصل'. This part discusses the commonalities between genus and differentia.
2
Extract the shared properties
Identify the three properties mentioned: (1) their nature can be predicated of a single species and others, (2) what is predicated of them as 'what it is' applies to subordinates with a different sense, (3) removing both removes the species (e.g., animality and rationality remove humanity).
Use the example of 'man' (insan) being removed when 'animal' and 'rational' are removed.
3
Compare with genus-and-species commonalities
Now look at the section 'وأما الجنس والنوع'. Note that genus and species share answering 'what is it?' and being prior to their subjects, which is different from genus-differentia properties.
4
Summarize the distinction
The commonalities of genus-differentia are tied to composition and removal, while genus-species share predicative roles. Summarize in one sentence how these sets differ.

وأما المشاركات الباقية بين كل واحد منها وحده وبين غيره فإنها متعددة لا بد للتعرض لما هو من المشهورات منها. أما الجنس والفصل فقد مرَّ الكلام فيهما كلاما جامعا يشير إلى كثير من المشاركات، ولأنهما يشتركان في أن لا يمتنع في طبيعة كل واحد منهما أن يحمل على النوع الواحد وغيره، فقد مرَّ الكلام فيه ويشتركان أيضًا في أن كل ما يحمل عليهما من طريق ما هو فذلك يحمل على ما تحتهما من طريق ما هو، ويُعنى بهذا الحمل غير ما يُعنى به في جواب ما هو . وكذلك في أن رفعهما هو رفع ما تحتهما من الأنواع فإذا رفعت الحيوانية والنطق رفع الإنسان لا محالة. وأما الجنس والنوع فإنهما يشتركان في كون كل واحد منهما مقولاً في جواب ما هو . وكذلك في أنهما يتقدمان على ما يحملان عليه وهو الذي له جنس ونوع، وفي أن كل واحد منهما ماهية كل واحد من جزئياته بالشركة. وأما الجنس والخاصة فإنهما يشتركان في أن الرسم الكامل لا يتركب إلا منهما فيكونا جزئية، ولأنهما يحملان على النوع دائمًا ويتبعانه كذلك. وأما الجنس مع العرض العام فإنهما يشتركان في كون كل واحد منهما مقولا على المختلفة بالحقائق ومفتقرا في الوجود إليها. وأما الفصل والنوع فإنهما يشتركان في كون كل واحد منهما مقولاً على المختلفة بالعدد فقط، ومحمولا على ما تحتهما بالسوية؛ وأما الفصل والخاصة فإنهما يشتركان في اختصاصهما بالنوع وفي كل واحد منهما مقولاً في جواب أي شيء ما هو؛ وأما الفصل والعرض العام فإنهما يشتركان في أنه يمكن أن يعم كل واحد منهما هذا النوع وغيره من الأنواع وفي أنهما على الدوام لموضوعاتهما؛ وهذا بالنسبة إلى غير المفارق. وأما النوع والخاصة فإنهما يشتركان في أن ينعكس كل واحد منهما على الآخر، فيقال كل إنسان ضحاك وكل ضحاك إنسان، وفي أنهما يوجدان معا لموضوعاتهما دائما. وأما النوع والعرض العام فإنهما يشتركان في أن لا توجد لهما أشياء كثيرة يشتركان فيها لبعد ما بينهما وفي أنه يمكن أن يكون كل واحد منهما غنيا عن الآخر في وجوده وهذا بالنسبة إلى غير المفارق. وأما الخاصة والعرض العام فقد مرَّ الكلام فيهما من قبل.
As for the remaining commonalities between each one of them individually and between others, they are numerous, and it is necessary to mention what is well-known from them. As for genus and differentia, discussion about them has already passed, a comprehensive discussion indicating many commonalities. Because they share in that it is not prevented in the nature of each of them to be predicated of the single species and others. Already the discussion passed, and they also share in that everything that is predicated of them in the way of 'what it is', that is predicated of what is under them in the way of 'what it is', and what is meant by this predication is other than what is meant in answer to 'what is it'. And similarly that their removal is the removal of what is under them of species: if animality and rationality are removed, man is removed inevitably. As for genus and species, they share in that each of them is said in answer to 'what is it'. And similarly that they are prior to what they are predicated of, which is that which has genus and species, and that each of them is the quiddity of each of its particulars by participation. As for genus and property, they share in that the complete description is composed only of them, so they become its part, and because they are always predicated of the species and follow it likewise. As for genus with general accident, they share in that each of them is said of things differing in realities and is dependent for existence upon them. As for differentia and species, they share in that each of them is said of things differing only in number, and are predicated of what is under them equally. As for differentia and property, they share in their being specific to the species and each of them is said in answer to 'which thing it is'. As for differentia and general accident, they share in that each of them can extend to this species and other species, and that they are permanent for their subjects; and this is with respect to what is not separable. As for species and property, they share in that each of them is convertible upon the other, so it is said: every man is laughing and every laughing is man, and that they exist together always for their subjects. As for species and general accident, they share in that there are not many things in which they share, due to the distance between them, and that each of them can be independent of the other in its existence, and this is with respect to what is not separable. As for property and general accident, discussion about them has already passed before.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the commonalities (mushtarakat) among the five universals: genus (jins), differentia (fasl), species (naw'), property (khassa), and general accident (arad 'amm). The author proceeds pair by pair, explaining the shared characteristics. For genus and differentia, they share that their nature can be predicated of a single species, and that whatever is predicated of them as 'what it is' applies to their subordinates, but with a different sense. They also share that removing them removes the subspecies, e.g., removing animality and rationality removes man. Genus and species share that both answer 'what is it?' and are prior to their subjects, and each is the quiddity of its particulars by participation. Genus and property share that a complete description (rasm) is composed only from them, and both are always predicated of the species. Genus and general accident share that they are predicated of things differing in reality and depend on them for existence. Differentia and species share that they are predicated only of things differing in number, and equally. Differentia and property share specificity to the species and answering 'which thing is it?'. Differentia and general accident share that they can extend to multiple species and are permanent (non-separable). Species and property share mutual convertibility (e.g., every human is laughing and vice versa) and coexistence. Species and general accident share that they have little in common and can exist independently. The author uses examples from the tradition, such as 'man is laughing' (al-insan dahhak) for species and property. The passage is a parsing exercise for students to understand the web of relations among the universals.

Commonalities of Property and General Accident with Other Universals
Author enumerates shared characteristics between each pair of the five universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the shared features of property (khassa) and general accident ('arad 'amm) with genus, differentia, and species.

Core Idea
Property is convertible with species; general accident is independent and extends beyond species.

Now we examine the commonalities involving property (khassa) and general accident ('arad 'amm) with the other universals. Notice how property is tightly linked to species through convertibility, while general accident is more independent. This helps in distinguishing essential from accidental predicates.

Guided Path
1
Find pairs involving property and general accident
In the Arabic, locate the sections: 'وأما الجنس والخاصة', 'وأما الفصل والخاصة', 'وأما النوع والخاصة', and the respective ones with general accident.
2
Extract property's commonalities with genus and differentia
From 'الجنس والخاصة': they share that a complete description (rasm) is composed of them, and both are always predicated of the species. From 'الفصل والخاصة': they share specificity to the species and answering 'which thing is it?'.
3
Focus on species-property convertibility
In 'النوع والخاصة', they share that each is convertible with the other, e.g., 'every man is laughing' and 'every laughing is man'. This is a defining feature of property.
4
Compare with general accident commonalities
From 'الجنس مع العرض العام' and 'الفصل مع العرض العام', note that general accident shares with genus being said of differing realities, and with differentia being permanent (for non-separable). From 'النوع والعرض العام', they share independence in existence.
5
Summarize the contrast between property and general accident
Property is convertible with species and always coexists; general accident is not convertible and can exist independently. State this in your own words.

The Principle of Mubayana: Difference Within Commonality
Author explains the principle of mubayana among the five universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the commonality and difference between any two of the five universals based on the principle of mubayana.

Core Idea
Each universal shares and differs simultaneously; difference is embedded within commonality.

The five universals—genus, species, differentia, property, and accident—are not isolated categories. Each contains both a shared feature (e.g., being a universal) and a distinctive feature that makes it unique. The principle of mubayana teaches us how these universals relate: they are necessarily both common and distinct. This lesson walks you through this principle using the example of genus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared property among all five universals
All five universals share the property of being 'universal' (kulli). This is the basis of their commonality.
2
Recognize the distinctive property that sets each apart
For genus, its distinctiveness is that it makes something a genus—a category that can be divided into species. For species, it is the subdivision under genus. Each universal has a unique defining role.
3
Apply the mubayana principle to a pair
Take genus and species. They share being universal, but differ in that genus is broader and predicable of multiple species, while species is narrower and predicable of individuals. The difference is always within the commonality.
4
Extend to groups of four, three, and two universals
The passage says: what four share, the fifth differs; what three share, the fourth and fifth differ, etc. Practically, if genus, species, differentia, and property share some aspect, accident differs in that aspect. Work out examples for exercise.
This step requires careful reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما «المباينات»؛ فالمباينة بينها في ضمن المشاركة، وقد كان كل واحد من الخمسة مشتملا على ما به المشاركة وعلى ما به المباينة أيضًا. فلو كان الجنس مثلاً مشاركًا لغيره في كونه كليا لكان مباينا له في غير كونه كليا وهو الذي يجعله جنسًا . وعلى هذا في الغير فتكون الخمسة متشاركة ومتباينة بالضرورة، وكذلك الأربعة وغيرها؛ وما يشترك فيه الأربعة أيضًا فالخامس مما يباينها فيه، وما يشترك فيه الثلاثة فالرابع والخامس مما يباينها فيه كذلك. وعلى هذا في الاثنين منها، وهذا ظاهر يعرف بالتأمل. ثم ما يكون من اللوازم ذكره في هذا الموضع فذلك ذكر المباينة بين كل واحد من الخمسة وحده وبين غيره. أما بين الجنس والفصل فالمباينة المشهورة بينهما هي أن الجنس أقدم من الفصل؛ وذلك لأن الجنس قد يوجد للفصل المعين وقد لا يوجد، والفصل إنما وجوده في الجنس فلا يوجد بدونه؛ ولذلك لا ترتفع طبيعة الجنس برفع طبيعة الفصل، وترتفع طبيعة الفصل برفع طبيعة الجنس. وقد يقال في هذا الموضع إن من الفصول ما لا يكون خارجًا عن طبيعة الجنس مثل الانقسام بمتساويين فإنه فصل الزوج وخارج عن العدد لكن الجواب سيلوح في موضع آخر. والثانية منها هو أن الجنس يحمل من طريق ما، وهو الفصل من طريق أي ما هو . وهذا من جملة ما لا يكون بانفراده
As for the differences: the difference between them is within the commonality, and each of the five contains that by which it shares and that by which it differs. So if, for example, genus shares with another in being a universal, it differs from it in its not being a universal, which is what makes it a genus. And likewise for the other. So the five are necessarily sharing and differing, and likewise the four and others. What the four share, the fifth differs from them in it, and what the three share, the fourth and fifth differ from them in it, and so on for two of them. This is apparent and known by reflection. Then among the necessary things to mention in this place is the mention of the difference between each of the five individually and others. As for between genus and differentia, the well-known difference between them is that genus is prior to differentia; because the genus may exist for the specific differentia and may not exist, but the differentia only exists in the genus, so it does not exist without it. Therefore the nature of the genus is not removed by removing the nature of the differentia, but the nature of the differentia is removed by removing the nature of the genus. It may be said in this place that among differentiae there are those that are not external to the nature of the genus, like division into equals, which is the differentia of even and external to number, but the answer will appear in another place. The second of them is that the genus is predicated in the way of 'what', and the differentia in the way of 'which thing it is'. This is among the things that are not alone.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage discusses the differences (mubayinat) between the five universals, starting with a general principle: each universal contains both a commonality and a difference. For example, genus shares the property of being a universal with others, but differs in what makes it specifically genus. The author then systematically describes how, as we move from five to fewer universals, the remaining ones share certain properties and differ in others. The main focus is on the difference between genus (jins) and differentia (fasl). The well-known difference is that genus is prior to differentia. Genus can exist without a specific differentia, but differentia cannot exist without its genus. For instance, the nature of genus (e.g., animal) is not removed by removing rationality, but rationality is removed if animal is removed. The author notes an objection: some differentiae, like 'being divisible into equals' for the number even, seem to be external to the genus (number) but promises a later answer. A second difference is the mode of predication: genus answers 'what is it?' while differentia answers 'which thing is it?'. This passage requires careful parsing of the priority relations and the distinction between essential and accidental predication.

الجنس
الفصل

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is true about the five universals?
A) They are entirely distinct with no common features.
B) Each universal contains both a common and a distinctive aspect.
C) Only genus and species share a commonality.
D) The differences are external to the commonality.

Key Takeaway
The five universals are necessarily both common and distinct; difference is always within a framework of shared properties.

Distinguishing Genus and Differentia: Priority and Predication
Author details the differences between genus and differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between genus (jins) and differentia (fasl) based on their priority relation and mode of predication.

Core Idea
Genus is prior to differentia and answers 'what is it?'; differentia depends on genus and answers 'which thing is it?'.

Among the five universals, the distinction between genus (jins) and differentia (fasl) is foundational. This lesson focuses on two key differences highlighted by the author: priority and mode of predication. Genus can exist without a particular differentia, but differentia cannot exist without its genus. Moreover, genus is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' (e.g., 'animal'), while differentia answers 'which thing is it?' (e.g., 'rational'). These rules are critical for constructing valid definitions (hadd).

Guided Path
1
Understand the priority of genus over differentia
The text states: 'الجنس أقدم من الفصل' (genus is prior to differentia). This means genus can exist without a specific differentia. For example, 'animal' (genus) can exist without 'rational' (differentia), but 'rational' cannot exist without 'animal'.
Think of genus as the foundation; differentia adds specificity.
2
Apply the removal test
The author says: the nature of genus is not removed by removing differentia, but differentia is removed by removing genus. So if you remove 'rational', 'animal' remains; if you remove 'animal', 'rational' disappears.
This test confirms ontological dependence.
3
Distinguish by mode of predication
Genus is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' (ما هو), while differentia answers 'which thing is it?' (أي ما هو). For instance, when asked 'What is it?', you say 'animal'; when asked 'Which animal?', you say 'rational'.
This distinction is essential for hadd: genus gives the category, differentia specifies.
4
Consider the exception about differentiae external to genus
The passage notes an objection: some differentiae, like 'divisible into equals' for even number, seem external to the genus 'number'. The author promises an answer later, but for now, recognize that not all differentiae are internal to the genus in the same way.
This is an advanced nuance; focus on the main rule.

Distinguishing Species from Property and Accident
Author lists commonalities between species and property, and between species and accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate species from property and accident based on convertibility and dependence relationships.

Core Idea
Species and property are convertible; species and accident are not.

In our study of the five universals, we have already encountered species, property, and accident. Now the author clarifies the logical relationships between them. Understanding these relationships is crucial because confusing a property with a species leads to incorrect definitions. Let's unpack the commonalities and differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two pairs being compared
The passage compares species with property, and species with accident. Notice that the author states two commonalities for each pair.
2
Analyze the first commonality for species and property
They share mutual convertibility: 'Every human is a laugher' and 'Every laugher is a human'. This means they are co-extensive. Verify that this is true for a given property and its species.
3
Analyze the second commonality for species and property
Both always coexist with their subjects. For any individual human, both humanity (species) and laughter (property) are always present. Recognize that this differs from accidents that may sometimes be absent.
4
Analyze the commonalities for species and accident
First, they share few inferior universals because of the conceptual distance between essence (species) and accident. Second, each can exist without the other. For example, 'human' can exist without 'moving' (if sitting still), and 'moving' can exist without 'human' (e.g., a stone).
5
Compare the two pairs
Notice that species and property are tightly linked (convertible and always together), while species and accident are loosely linked (rarely share inferiors, independent existence). This shows that property is co-extensive with species but not essential, while accident is neither co-extensive nor essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النوع / والخاصة فإنهما يشتركان في أن ينعكس كل واحد منهما على الآخر، فيقال كل إنسان ضحاك وكل ضحاك إنسان، وفي أنهما يوجدان معا لموضوعاتهما دائما. وأما النوع والعرض العام فإنهما يشتركان في أن لا توجد لهما أشياء كثيرة يشتركان فيها لبعد ما بينهما وفي أنه يمكن أن يكون كل واحد منهما غنيا عن الآخر في وجوده وهذا بالنسبة إلى غير المفارق. وأما الخاصة والعرض العام فقد مرَّ الكلام فيهما من قبل.
As for species and property, they share that each one is convertible upon the other, so it is said: 'Every human is a laugher, and every laugher is a human', and that they both exist together for their subjects always. As for species and accident, they share that there are not many things that they share, due to the distance between them, and that each one can be independent of the other in its existence, and this with respect to non-separable (accident). As for property and accident, discussion of them has already passed.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage enumerates the commonalities between pairs of the five universals. First, it states that species (النوع) and property (الخاصة) share two features: (1) mutual convertibility – e.g., 'human' and 'laugher' are co-extensive, so every human is a laugher and vice versa; (2) they always coexist with their subjects (e.g., humanity and laughter always accompany the individual human). Second, it states that species and accident (العرض العام) share two features: (1) they have few common inferiors because they are far apart in meaning – species is essential, accident is accidental; (2) they can exist independently of each other, meaning a species can exist without that particular accident, and vice versa. The passage ends by noting that property and accident have already been discussed earlier.

الخاصة
العرض العام

D) Every animal is a body but not every body is an animal – this shows genus-species relationship.

Key Takeaway
Species and property are convertible and always coexist, while species and accident are not convertible and can exist independently.

Participation and Distinction of the Five Universals
Author explains how each universal simultaneously participates with others and is distinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the aspect of participation and the aspect of distinction for any of the five universals and explain their relationship.

Core Idea
The five universals share universality but differ in their specific defining roles.

The five universals—genus, species, differentia, property, and accident—are not isolated categories. Each has a dual nature: it participates with the others in being a universal, yet it is distinguished by a unique function. This passage explains this intrinsic relationship, which is essential for precise definitions in Islamic logic and other sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components in each universal
The text says each universal contains 'what it participates in' (e.g., universality) and 'what it is distinguished by' (its specific role). For example, genus participates in universality but is distinguished by being predicated of many species differing in essence.
2
Analyze the example of genus
The author uses genus: it shares universality with other universals, but its distinguishing feature makes it genus (predication of many species). Understand that this distinguishing feature is what defines its essence.
This pattern applies to all five.
3
Extend to larger subsets
Consider any four universals: they share something (e.g., being predicated of many? Actually, the text says 'what the four share, the fifth is distinct in it'. So for any subset, the remaining universals are distinct in that shared property. Reflect on this mutual relationship.
The author says it's clear upon reflection.
4
Verify with examples from Islamic sciences
Take the definition of 'wudu' (ablution) as 'washing specific parts with water'. Here, 'washing' is a genus (shared with ghusl), and 'specific parts' is the differentia. Notice how they participate in being universals but are distinguished by their specific roles.
This shows the practical relevance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما «المباينات»؛ فالمباينة بينها في ضمن المشاركة، وقد كان كل واحد من الخمسة مشتملا على ما به المشاركة وعلى ما به المباينة أيضًا. فلو كان الجنس مثلاً مشاركًا لغيره في كونه كليا لكان مباينا له في غير كونه كليا وهو الذي يجعله جنسًا . وعلى هذا في الغير فتكون الخمسة متشاركة ومتباينة بالضرورة، وكذلك الأربعة وغيرها؛ وما يشترك فيه الأربعة أيضًا فالخامس مما يباينها فيه، وما يشترك فيه الثلاثة فالرابع والخامس مما يباينها فيه كذلك. وعلى هذا في الاثنين منها، وهذا ظاهر يعرف بالتأمل.
As for distinctions, the distinction among them is within participation. And each one of the five includes that by which it participates and that by which it is distinguished. So if the genus, for example, participates with another in its being a universal, it is distinguished from it in what is not its being a universal, which is what makes it a genus. And likewise for the others. Thus the five are necessarily participating and distinct. And similarly for the four and others; what the four share, the fifth is distinct in it, and what the three share, the fourth and fifth are distinct in them likewise. And similarly for two of them. This is clear and known by reflection.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage discusses the fact that distinctions (المباينات) among the five universals exist within commonalities (المشاركة). Each universal has two aspects: one that it shares with other universals (e.g., being a universal) and one that makes it unique (e.g., being a genus as opposed to species). For example, genus shares with species the property of being a universal, but it differs from species in that it is predicated of multiple species. This pattern holds for any subset: the four universals share something, and the fifth differs in that respect; three share, and the other two differ, and so on. The author concludes that this is self-evident upon contemplation.

Key Terms
المباينة
المشاركة
الجنس

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) They are identical in all respects.
D) Only genus and species share anything.

Key Takeaway
Each of the five universals combines a shared property (universality) with a unique distinguishing feature that defines its specific logical role.

Three Distinctions Between Genus and Differentia
Author details three points of distinction between genus and differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish genus from differentia using the three criteria of priority, mode of predication, and matter-form analogy.

Core Idea
Genus is prior, answers 'what is it?', and is like matter to differentia's form.

To master the five universals, you must be able to tell genus and differentia apart. This passage from the logic text presents three clear criteria: existential priority, the type of question they answer, and an analogy with matter and form. Understanding these distinctions is essential for constructing correct definitions in any Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion of priority
The author states genus can exist without a specific differentia, but not vice versa. Find that statement and note how priority shows dependence.
This is the most fundamental distinction.
2
Identify the criterion of predicative question
Genus answers 'what is it?' while differentia answers 'which thing is it?'. Locate the author's discussion and note the caution that this distinction alone does not prove separation.
The author refutes a view that differentia can answer 'what is it?'.
3
Identify the matter-form analogy
The author compares genus to matter and differentia to form. Find the analogy and note its limits: matter is not predicated of composite, but genus is.
Also note that genus contains opposite differentiae simultaneously, unlike matter.

ثم ما يكون من اللوازم ذكره في هذا الموضع فذلك ذكر المباينة بين كل واحد من الخمسة وحده وبين غيره. أما بين الجنس والفصل فالمباينة المشهورة بينهما هي أن الجنس أقدم من الفصل؛ وذلك لأن الجنس قد يوجد للفصل المعين وقد لا يوجد، والفصل إنما وجوده في الجنس فلا يوجد بدونه؛ ولذلك لا ترتفع طبيعة الجنس برفع طبيعة الفصل، وترتفع طبيعة الفصل برفع طبيعة الجنس. وقد يقال في هذا الموضع إن من الفصول ما لا يكون خارجًا عن طبيعة الجنس مثل الانقسام بمتساويين فإنه فصل الزوج وخارج عن العدد لكن الجواب سيلوح في موضع آخر. والثانية منها هو أن الجنس يحمل من طريق ما، وهو الفصل من طريق أي ما هو . وهذا من جملة ما لا يكون بانفراده دالا على المباينة، فإن الاختلاف في الوصفين لا يكون دالا على التباين بين الشيئين؛ بل يجب أن يكون بينهما قوة السلب ولا يقال كون الجنس مقولا في جواب ما هو لا يمنع أن يكون مقولا في جواب أي ما هو كما مرَّ من قبل فإن ذلك لا يمكن وإن ذهب إليه قوم. وأما قول البعض من الفضلاء إن الفصل قد يكون مقولا في جواب ما هو أيضًا في بعض من الأشياء دون البعض، فإن الفصول ربما كانت مناسبات وإضافات إلى أفعال وانفعالات، فذلك فاسد. فإن ما كان من الفصول تجري هذا المجرى فلا يكون فصلا مقوما بل يكون من اللوازم والثالث هو أن الجنس كالمادة والفصل كالصورة وإنما قيل كالمادة؛ إذ المادة لا تحمل على المركب مثل ما يحمل الجسم عليه وهو حمل أنه هو . وإن المادة الموضوعة لصورتين متقابلتين لا ينسب إليهما بالفعل إلا في [۳۳] زمانين والجنس يكون مشتملا على الفصلين المتقابلين في زمان واحد. وقد قيل في أنه كالمادة إن طبيعته عند الذهن قبالة للفصل فإذا لحقه الفصل صار شيئًا مقوما بالفعل كما هو حال المادة عند الصورة، وإذن الجنس للفصل كالمادة للصورة فالفصل للجنس كالصورة للمادة.
Then what is necessary to mention in this place is the mention of the distinction between each one of the five and the others. As for between genus and differentia, the well-known distinction between them is that the genus is prior to the differentia; because the genus may exist for a specific differentia and may not exist, while the differentia only exists in the genus, so it does not exist without it. Therefore the nature of the genus is not removed by removing the nature of the differentia, but the nature of the differentia is removed by removing the nature of the genus. It may be said in this place that some differentiae are not outside the nature of the genus, like division into equals – it is a differentia of even number and is outside number, but the answer will appear elsewhere. The second is that the genus is predicated in the way of 'what is it', while the differentia is predicated in the way of 'which thing is it'. This is among what does not by itself indicate distinction, because the difference in two descriptions does not indicate the distinction between two things; rather there must be a power of negation between them. And it cannot be said that the genus being said in answer to 'what is it' prevents it from being said in answer to 'which thing is it' as previously mentioned, for that is impossible even if some people hold it. As for the statement of some of the virtuosi that the differentia may also be said in answer to 'what is it' in some things without others, because differentiae may be relations and additions to actions and passions, that is invalid. For what among differentiae runs in this course is not a constitutive differentia but rather from among the concomitants. The third is that the genus is like matter and the differentia like form. It is called 'like matter' because matter is not predicated of the composite as body is predicated of it, which is a predication of identity. And matter that is subject to two opposite forms is not attributed to them actually except in two times, while the genus contains two opposite differentiae at one time. It has been said that it is like matter because its nature in the mind stands opposite the differentia; when the differentia attaches to it, it becomes something actually constitutive as is the case with matter with form. Thus the genus is to the differentia as matter is to form, and the differentia is to the genus as form is to matter.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage gives three main distinctions between genus and differentia. First, genus is prior to differentia in existence: genus can exist without that particular differentia (e.g., animal without rationality), but differentia cannot exist without its genus. Thus if you remove differentia, genus remains; if you remove genus, differentia disappears. An objection is noted about differentiae that seem outside the genus (e.g., 'even' for number), but the author defers the answer. Second, genus answers 'what is it?' (ما هو) while differentia answers 'which thing is it?' (أي ما هو). However, the author warns that this difference in category of predication does not by itself prove they are distinct things unless accompanied by a power of negation (i.e., one can be negated of the other). The author refutes a view that some differentiae can answer 'what is it?', arguing that such differentiae are not constitutive but merely concomitant. Third, genus is like matter and differentia like form. Matter is not predicated of the composite (e.g., 'body' is not predicated of 'human' in the same way), but genus is predicated of species. However, the analogy holds in that genus receives differentiae like matter receives forms, and genus can contain opposite differentiae simultaneously unlike matter which receives opposite forms at different times. The passage concludes by solidifying the analogy.

C) Differentia is always more important than genus.
D) Genus is predicated of differentia.

Key Takeaway
Genus and differentia are not interchangeable; genus is independent and answers 'what is it?', differentia depends on genus and answers 'which one?'.

Three Distinctions Between Genus and Species
Author enumerates three ways genus differs from species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three ways genus differs from species and apply these distinctions to evaluate hierarchical relationships in a Porphyrian tree.

Core Idea
Genus is broader, prior, and more encompassing than species.

In Islamic logic, the five universals include genus (jins) and species (naw'). Understanding how they differ is essential for constructing correct definitions. This passage enumerates three key distinctions that clarify their hierarchical relationship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three distinctions
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. The author mentions three ways genus and species differ. List them in your own words.
2
Understand the first distinction: universal predication
Genus is predicated universally of species (e.g., 'animal' of 'human'), but species cannot be predicated universally of genus (e.g., 'human' is not true of all animals).
3
Understand the second distinction: priority in nature
The nature of genus is prior: its existence does not imply species (animal does not imply human), but its non-existence implies non-existence of species (if no animal, then no human).
4
Understand the third distinction: extension
Genus encompasses species and others (e.g., animal includes humans and horses), while species does not encompass genus and others (human does not include animals).
5
Apply to an example
Test these distinctions using a concrete pair from Islamic science, such as 'substance' (jism) and 'body' (jasad), or 'living thing' (hayy) and 'human' (insan).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بين الجنس والنوع فالمباينة بينهما بوجوه ؛ منها: أن الجنس يحمل على النوع بالتواطؤ حملا كليا والنوع لا يحمل على الجنس حملا كليا. والثانية منها هي أن طبيعة الجنس أقدم من طبيعة النوع يعني لا يلزم من طبيعة الجنس طبيعة النوع ؛ بل يلزم من ارتفاعها ارتفاع طبيعة النوع والثالثة أن الجنس من جملة ما يعم النوع وغيره، والنوع لا يكون من جملة ما يعم الجنس وغيره؛ بل لا يمكن أن يكون كذلك.
As for between genus and species, the distinction between them is in several aspects: one is that genus is predicated of species by way of equivocity (?) [alt. reading: by way of universality?] a universal predication, while species is not predicated of genus as a universal predication. The second is that the nature of genus is prior to the nature of species, meaning that the nature of species does not necessarily follow from the nature of genus, but the rise (removal) of genus necessitates the rise (removal) of the nature of species. The third is that genus is part of what encompasses species and others, while species is not part of what encompasses genus and others; rather it cannot be like that.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage lists three distinctions between genus and species. First, genus is predicated universally of species (e.g., 'animal' of 'human'), but species cannot be predicated universally of genus (e.g., 'human' is not true of all animals). Second, the nature of genus is prior to species: existence of genus does not imply existence of species (e.g., animal does not imply human), but the non-existence of genus implies non-existence of species (if no animal, then no human). Third, genus has a wider extension covering species and other species, while species does not cover genus and other genera; species is narrower. This highlights the hierarchical relationship in the Porphyrian tree.

النوع
التواطؤ

Key Takeaway
Genus and species differ in predication, priority, and extension — three criteria that structure the logical tree of definitions.

Distinction Between Genus and Property
Author begins enumeration of distinctions between genus and property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish genus from property using the criterion of convertibility (al-ʿaks).

Core Idea
Genus is not convertible with species; property is convertible.

This lesson unpacks the first distinction between genus (al-jins) and property (al-khāṣṣah) made by the author. The key difference lies in convertibility: genus is predicated of its species without the reverse being true, while property and species are mutually predicable. Grasping this will sharpen your understanding of the five universals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two universals being compared
Find 'al-jins' (genus) and 'al-khāṣṣah' (property) in the Arabic. Note they are both universals, but the passage distinguishes them.
2
Understand the criterion: 'without converse' (min ghayr ʿaks)
The phrase 'min ghayr ʿaks' indicates that genus is not convertible with its species. For example, 'animal' is said of 'human', but 'human' is not said of 'animal'.
The opposite is true for property: it is convertible.
3
Apply the convertibility test to examples
Test 'laugher' as a property of human: 'human is laugher' and 'laugher is human' are both true (co-extensive). If it were a genus, only one direction would hold.
4
Note the implication of 'also' (ayḍan)
The author says 'also' (ayḍan) and 'from many aspects' (bi-wujūh), indicating this is one of several distinctions. Further distinctions may follow beyond this page.
This passage is incomplete; more criteria are expected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بين الجنس والخاصة فالمباينة بينهما بوجوه أيضًا منها أن الجنس يحمل على كل واحد من الأنواع حملا كليا من غير عكس بخلاف الخاصة. فإنها كما تحمل على النوع فكذلك النوع عليها.
As for between genus and property, the distinction between them is in several aspects also, one of which is that genus is predicated of each one of the species in a universal predication without converse, unlike property. For just as it is predicated of the species, so the species is [predicated] upon it.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage begins the discussion of distinctions between genus and property. The first distinction mentioned is that genus is predicated universally of all its species (e.g., 'animal' of 'human', 'horse'), but this predication is not convertible: species is not predicated of genus. In contrast, property is convertible with its species: e.g., 'human' and 'laugher' are co-extensive. The phrasing 'as it is predicated of the species, so the species is predicated upon it' indicates mutual predication. The text implies there are more distinctions, but the page ends here.
