Distinguishing Genus from Differentia
Author explains the differences between jins and fasl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate genus (jins) from differentia (fasl) based on their predicational roles, the matter-form analogy, and refute the misconception that differentia can answer 'what is it?'.

Core Idea
Genus answers 'what is it?'; differentia answers 'which one is it?' — like matter to form.

The author begins by emphasizing that true opposition between universals requires a power of negation, not mere difference in descriptions. He then clarifies the fundamental distinction between genus and differentia: genus is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' (mā huwa), while differentia answers 'which one is it?' (ayy mā huwa). Some scholars mistakenly thought differentia could also answer 'what is it?', but the author refutes this, explaining that such supposed differentiae are merely concomitants. He then provides a powerful analogy: genus is like matter, potential and receptive, while differentia is like form that actualizes the species.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary distinction
Find where the author states that genus is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' and differentia in answer to 'which one is it?'. This is the core logical difference.
The Arabic phrases: مقولا في جواب ما هو for genus, and مقولا في جواب أي ما هو for differentia.
2
Analyze the critique against overlap
The author refutes those who claim differentia can sometimes answer 'what is it?'. He says such differentiae are not constitutive but merely relative concomitants (للوازم).
This distinction protects the purity of the five universals.
3
Examine the matter-form analogy
Genus is like matter (كالمادة) because it is potential and receives differentia. Differentia is like form (كالصورة) that actualizes the species. Unlike matter, genus can encompass opposite differentiae simultaneously.
The analogy highlights the logical priority of genus.
4
Consider the implications for definition
Because genus and differentia play distinct roles, a complete definition (hadd) must include both. If differentia were to answer 'what is it?', definitions would become circular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دالا على المباينة، فإن الاختلاف في الوصفين لا يكون دالا على التباين بين الشيئين؛ بل يجب أن يكون بينهما قوة السلب ولا يقال كون الجنس مقولا في جواب ما هو لا يمنع أن يكون مقولا في جواب أي ما هو كما مرَّ من قبل فإن ذلك لا يمكن وإن ذهب إليه قوم. وأما قول البعض من الفضلاء إن الفصل قد يكون مقولا في جواب ما هو أيضًا في بعض من الأشياء دون البعض، فإن الفصول ربما كانت مناسبات وإضافات إلى أفعال وانفعالات، فذلك فاسد. فإن ما كان من الفصول تجري هذا المجرى فلا يكون فصلا مقوما بل يكون من اللوازم والثالث هو أن الجنس كالمادة والفصل كالصورة وإنما قيل كالمادة؛ إذ المادة لا تحمل على المركب مثل ما يحمل الجسم عليه وهو حمل أنه هو . وإن المادة الموضوعة لصورتين متقابلتين لا ينسب إليهما بالفعل إلا في [۳۳] زمانين والجنس يكون مشتملا على الفصلين المتقابلين في زمان واحد. وقد قيل في أنه كالمادة إن طبيعته عند الذهن قبالة للفصل فإذا لحقه الفصل صار شيئًا مقوما بالفعل كما هو حال المادة عند الصورة، وإذن الجنس للفصل كالمادة للصورة فالفصل للجنس كالصورة للمادة.
وأما بين الجنس والنوع فالمباينة بينهما بوجوه ؛ منها: أن الجنس يحمل على النوع بالتواطؤ حملا كليا والنوع لا يحمل على الجنس حملا كليا. والثانية منها هي أن طبيعة الجنس أقدم من طبيعة النوع يعني لا يلزم من طبيعة الجنس طبيعة النوع ؛ بل يلزم من ارتفاعها ارتفاع طبيعة النوع والثالثة أن الجنس من جملة ما يعم النوع وغيره، والنوع لا يكون من جملة ما يعم الجنس وغيره؛ بل لا يمكن أن يكون كذلك. وأما بين الجنس والخاصة فالمباينة بينهما بوجوه أيضًا منها أن الجنس يحمل على كل واحد من الأنواع حملا كليا من غير عكس بخلاف الخاصة. فإنها كما تحمل على النوع فكذلك النوع عليها.
والثانية أن الجنس متقدم بالذات والخاصة متأخر لما أنها يحدث مع حدوث النوع. والثالثة أن الجنس يحوي أنواعًا والخاصة نوعا واحدا ليس إلا. وأما بين الجنس والعرض العام فمنها أن الجنس لا يحمل على الأشياء تحته إلا بالسوية، والأعراض ربما حملت لا بالسوية. فإنه ليس جميع الأعراض يحمل لا بالسوية كالمثلث والمربع مثلا. والثانية أن الأعراض توجد في الأشخاص على القصد الأول، والأنواع والأجناس لا توجد على القصد الأول. والثالثة أن الجنس قبل النوع والنوع قبل العرض العام لكنها لا تكون مخصوصة بهما؛ إذ هي موجودة بين الجنس والخاصة أيضًا كما إذا قلت هذا ذاتي وذلك غير ذاتي مثلا.
Indicating opposition, for difference in the two descriptions does not indicate disparity between the two things; rather there must be between them the power of negation. And it is not said that genus being predicated in answer to 'what is it?' does not prevent it from being predicated in answer to 'which one is it?' as was mentioned before, for that is not possible even if some people have held it. As for the saying of some of the virtuous that the differentia may be predicated in answer to 'what is it?' in some things and not others, because differentia may be relative and additions to actions and passions, that is corrupt. For what is such among differentia is not a constitutive differentia but rather from the concomitants. And the third is that genus is like matter and differentia like form; it is only said 'like matter' because matter is not predicated of the composite in the way body is predicated of it, which is a predication that it is it. And matter placed under two opposite forms is not actually related to them except in [two] times, while genus encompasses the two opposite differentia in one time. And it has been said concerning it being like matter that its nature in the mind is opposite to differentia; so when differentia attaches to it, it becomes something actually constitutive, just as is the case of matter with form. Thus genus is to differentia as matter is to form, and differentia is to genus as form is to matter. As for between genus and species, there is opposition between them in ways: Among them: genus is predicated of species by equivocity as a universal predication, and species is not predicated of genus as a universal predication. The second is that the nature of genus is prior to the nature of species, meaning that the nature of species does not necessarily follow from the nature of genus; rather the elevation of the nature of species necessarily follows from the elevation of genus. The third is that genus is among what encompasses species and others, while species is not among what encompasses genus and others; rather it cannot be so. As for between genus and property, there is opposition between them also in ways: among them is that genus is predicated of every one of the species as a universal predication without converse, unlike property. For just as it is predicated of the species, so too the species is predicated of it. The second is that genus is prior by essence and property is posterior, because it comes into existence with the coming into existence of the species. The third is that genus contains species, while property contains only one species. As for between genus and common accident: among them is that genus is not predicated of the things under it except equally, while accidents are sometimes predicated unequally. For not all accidents are predicated unequally, like triangle and square for example. The second is that accidents exist in individuals as first intention, while species and genera do not exist as first intention. The third is that genus is before species and species is before common accident, but they are not specific to them; rather they exist between genus and property as well, as when you say 'this is essential and that is accidental' for example.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a detailed comparison of the universal 'genus' (jins) with four other universals: differentia (fasl), species (naw'), property (khassa), and common accident ('arad 'amm). The author systematically enumerates multiple points of difference for each pair. The passage begins with a critique of those who mistakenly think differentia can be predicated in answer to 'what is it?' (mā huwa) just like genus. The author clarifies that a true constitutive differentia must be predicated in answer to 'which one is it?' (ayy mā huwa), and any differentia that behaves otherwise is merely a concomitant, not a constitutive differentia. Then the author draws an analogy: genus is like matter (mādda) and differentia like form (sūra), explaining that genus precedes differentia logically and can encompass opposite differentia potentially, while matter can only receive opposite forms in different times. Moving to genus and species, three differences are given: (1) genus is predicated of species universally but not vice versa; (2) the nature of genus is prior, meaning that if genus is removed, species is removed; (3) genus covers more than species. For genus and property: (1) genus is predicated of all species universally without converse, while property is reciprocal; (2) genus is prior by essence, property posterior; (3) genus contains multiple species, property only one. For genus and common accident: (1) genus predicates equally under it, accidents may not (but some accidents do predicate equally, like triangle and square); (2) accidents exist in individuals as first intention, genera and species do not; (3) genus is prior to species and species prior to common accident. The author notes that these distinctions are not exclusive to these pairs but also apply elsewhere, e.g., between genus and property, the 'essential vs. accidental' distinction is similar.

Key Terms
الجنس
الفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

What is the primary distinction between genus and differentia according to the passage?
A) Genus is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' and differentia in answer to 'which one is it?'
B) Genus is like form and differentia like matter
C) Genus and differentia are convertible in predication
D) Differentia can also answer 'what is it?' under certain conditions

Key Takeaway
Genus and differentia are distinguished by the type of question they answer: genus answers 'what is it?', differentia answers 'which one is it?'; they function like matter and form.

The Five Universals
intermediate
7 min
Distinguishing Genus from Species
Author enumerates three differences between jins and naw'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish genus (jins) from species (naw') using three criteria: predicative scope, ontological priority, and extension.

Core Idea
Genus is broader, predicated of species, prior in nature, and covers multiple species.

After comparing genus with differentia, the author turns to its relationship with species. He enumerates three precise differences. First, genus is predicated of species universally, but the reverse is not true. Second, the nature of genus is prior: if the genus is removed, the species is necessarily removed, but not vice versa. Third, genus encompasses more than one species, whereas a species stands under its genus uniquely. These criteria help in constructing proper hierarchies in definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first difference: predicative scope
The author states that genus is predicated of species by equivocity (بالتواطؤ) as a universal predication, but species is not predicated of genus. This means genus applies to all its species directly, but not the other way.
Example: 'animal' is predicated of 'human', but 'human' is not predicated of 'animal'.
2
Understand the second difference: priority
The nature of genus is prior to the nature of species. This means that the existence of species depends on genus, but not the reverse. If you remove genus, species disappears; removing species does not eliminate the genus.
This is a logical priority, not temporal.
3
Grasp the third difference: extension
Genus includes more than just one species; it encompasses all species under it. A species is specific and cannot cover other species or genus.
For instance, 'animal' includes 'human', 'horse', etc.; 'human' only includes individuals, not other species.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دالا على المباينة، فإن الاختلاف في الوصفين لا يكون دالا على التباين بين الشيئين؛ بل يجب أن يكون بينهما قوة السلب ولا يقال كون الجنس مقولا في جواب ما هو لا يمنع أن يكون مقولا في جواب أي ما هو كما مرَّ من قبل فإن ذلك لا يمكن وإن ذهب إليه قوم. وأما قول البعض من الفضلاء إن الفصل قد يكون مقولا في جواب ما هو أيضًا في بعض من الأشياء دون البعض، فإن الفصول ربما كانت مناسبات وإضافات إلى أفعال وانفعالات، فذلك فاسد. فإن ما كان من الفصول تجري هذا المجرى فلا يكون فصلا مقوما بل يكون من اللوازم والثالث هو أن الجنس كالمادة والفصل كالصورة وإنما قيل كالمادة؛ إذ المادة لا تحمل على المركب مثل ما يحمل الجسم عليه وهو حمل أنه هو . وإن المادة الموضوعة لصورتين متقابلتين لا ينسب إليهما بالفعل إلا في [۳۳] زمانين والجنس يكون مشتملا على الفصلين المتقابلين في زمان واحد. وقد قيل في أنه كالمادة إن طبيعته عند الذهن قبالة للفصل فإذا لحقه الفصل صار شيئًا مقوما بالفعل كما هو حال المادة عند الصورة، وإذن الجنس للفصل كالمادة للصورة فالفصل للجنس كالصورة للمادة.
وأما بين الجنس والنوع فالمباينة بينهما بوجوه ؛ منها: أن الجنس يحمل على النوع بالتواطؤ حملا كليا والنوع لا يحمل على الجنس حملا كليا. والثانية منها هي أن طبيعة الجنس أقدم من طبيعة النوع يعني لا يلزم من طبيعة الجنس طبيعة النوع ؛ بل يلزم من ارتفاعها ارتفاع طبيعة النوع والثالثة أن الجنس من جملة ما يعم النوع وغيره، والنوع لا يكون من جملة ما يعم الجنس وغيره؛ بل لا يمكن أن يكون كذلك. وأما بين الجنس والخاصة فالمباينة بينهما بوجوه أيضًا منها أن الجنس يحمل على كل واحد من الأنواع حملا كليا من غير عكس بخلاف الخاصة. فإنها كما تحمل على النوع فكذلك النوع عليها.
والثانية أن الجنس متقدم بالذات والخاصة متأخر لما أنها يحدث مع حدوث النوع. والثالثة أن الجنس يحوي أنواعًا والخاصة نوعا واحدا ليس إلا. وأما بين الجنس والعرض العام فمنها أن الجنس لا يحمل على الأشياء تحته إلا بالسوية، والأعراض ربما حملت لا بالسوية. فإنه ليس جميع الأعراض يحمل لا بالسوية كالمثلث والمربع مثلا. والثانية أن الأعراض توجد في الأشخاص على القصد الأول، والأنواع والأجناس لا توجد على القصد الأول. والثالثة أن الجنس قبل النوع والنوع قبل العرض العام لكنها لا تكون مخصوصة بهما؛ إذ هي موجودة بين الجنس والخاصة أيضًا كما إذا قلت هذا ذاتي وذلك غير ذاتي مثلا.
Indicating opposition, for difference in the two descriptions does not indicate disparity between the two things; rather there must be between them the power of negation. And it is not said that genus being predicated in answer to 'what is it?' does not prevent it from being predicated in answer to 'which one is it?' as was mentioned before, for that is not possible even if some people have held it. As for the saying of some of the virtuous that the differentia may be predicated in answer to 'what is it?' in some things and not others, because differentia may be relative and additions to actions and passions, that is corrupt. For what is such among differentia is not a constitutive differentia but rather from the concomitants. And the third is that genus is like matter and differentia like form; it is only said 'like matter' because matter is not predicated of the composite in the way body is predicated of it, which is a predication that it is it. And matter placed under two opposite forms is not actually related to them except in [two] times, while genus encompasses the two opposite differentia in one time. And it has been said concerning it being like matter that its nature in the mind is opposite to differentia; so when differentia attaches to it, it becomes something actually constitutive, just as is the case of matter with form. Thus genus is to differentia as matter is to form, and differentia is to genus as form is to matter. As for between genus and species, there is opposition between them in ways: Among them: genus is predicated of species by equivocity as a universal predication, and species is not predicated of genus as a universal predication. The second is that the nature of genus is prior to the nature of species, meaning that the nature of species does not necessarily follow from the nature of genus; rather the elevation of the nature of species necessarily follows from the elevation of genus. The third is that genus is among what encompasses species and others, while species is not among what encompasses genus and others; rather it cannot be so. As for between genus and property, there is opposition between them also in ways: among them is that genus is predicated of every one of the species as a universal predication without converse, unlike property. For just as it is predicated of the species, so too the species is predicated of it. The second is that genus is prior by essence and property is posterior, because it comes into existence with the coming into existence of the species. The third is that genus contains species, while property contains only one species. As for between genus and common accident: among them is that genus is not predicated of the things under it except equally, while accidents are sometimes predicated unequally. For not all accidents are predicated unequally, like triangle and square for example. The second is that accidents exist in individuals as first intention, while species and genera do not exist as first intention. The third is that genus is before species and species is before common accident, but they are not specific to them; rather they exist between genus and property as well, as when you say 'this is essential and that is accidental' for example.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a detailed comparison of the universal 'genus' (jins) with four other universals: differentia (fasl), species (naw'), property (khassa), and common accident ('arad 'amm). The author systematically enumerates multiple points of difference for each pair. The passage begins with a critique of those who mistakenly think differentia can be predicated in answer to 'what is it?' (mā huwa) just like genus. The author clarifies that a true constitutive differentia must be predicated in answer to 'which one is it?' (ayy mā huwa), and any differentia that behaves otherwise is merely a concomitant, not a constitutive differentia. Then the author draws an analogy: genus is like matter (mādda) and differentia like form (sūra), explaining that genus precedes differentia logically and can encompass opposite differentia potentially, while matter can only receive opposite forms in different times. Moving to genus and species, three differences are given: (1) genus is predicated of species universally but not vice versa; (2) the nature of genus is prior, meaning that if genus is removed, species is removed; (3) genus covers more than species. For genus and property: (1) genus is predicated of all species universally without converse, while property is reciprocal; (2) genus is prior by essence, property posterior; (3) genus contains multiple species, property only one. For genus and common accident: (1) genus predicates equally under it, accidents may not (but some accidents do predicate equally, like triangle and square); (2) accidents exist in individuals as first intention, genera and species do not; (3) genus is prior to species and species prior to common accident. The author notes that these distinctions are not exclusive to these pairs but also apply elsewhere, e.g., between genus and property, the 'essential vs. accidental' distinction is similar.

Key Terms
الجنس
النوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about genus and species?
A) Species is predicated of genus universally
B) Genus is prior in nature to species
C) Genus and species have the same extension
D) Species encompasses genus and others

Key Takeaway
Genus is predicated of species, is prior in nature, and has wider extension; species is subordinate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the definition 'al-khamr (wine) is a liquid intoxicant', identify the genus and species. Apply the three differences to verify that the relationship is correctly hierarchical.
?
Explain why 'human' cannot be a genus for 'animal' using the criteria of priority and extension.

The Five Universals
intermediate
10 min
Distinguishing Genus from Property and Common Accident
Author explains differences between jins and khassa and jins and 'arad 'amm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish genus (jins) from property (khassa) and common accident ('arad 'amm) using criteria of converseness, essentiality, priority, and scope.

Core Idea
Property is convertible with species but not essential; common accident is non-essential and may be unequal in predication.

The author completes his comparison of genus with the remaining two universals: property and common accident. For property, he gives three differences: genus is predicated of all its species without converse, genus is prior by essence, and genus contains multiple species while property only one. For common accident, he gives three more: genus predicates equally under it while some accidents are unequal, accidents (but not genera) exist in individuals as first intention, and genus is prior to species which is prior to common accident. These distinctions are vital for accurately categorizing terms in any science.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish genus from property
The author lists three differences: (1) genus is predicated of all species universally without converse (غیر عكس), while property is convertible (e.g., 'laughing' and 'human' are mutually predicable). (2) Genus is prior by essence; property comes into existence with the species. (3) Genus contains multiple species; property belongs to only one species.
Example: 'laughing' is a property of 'human' because only humans laugh, but 'human' is not predicated of 'laughing' in the same way.
2
Distinguish genus from common accident
Three differences: (1) Genus is predicated equally of all species under it (بالسوية), while some accidents are predicated unequally (e.g., 'white' may apply more to snow than to milk). However, the author notes that not all accidents are unequal (e.g., triangle and square both equally 'shape'). (2) Accidents exist in individuals as first intention (على القصد الأول), meaning they are directly predicated of individuals, while genera and species are predicated of individuals only through the species. (3) Genus is prior to species, and species prior to common accident.
This shows that common accident is more remote from the essence of the species.
3
Recognize the nuance about equal predication
The author points out that some accidents like 'triangle' and 'square' are predicated equally of their subjects (e.g., both are equally 'shape'), so equality of predication does not guarantee a genus. One must look at other criteria.
This nuance prevents confusing certain accidents with genera.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed comparison of the universal 'genus' (jins) with four other universals: differentia (fasl), species (naw'), property (khassa), and common accident ('arad 'amm). The author systematically enumerates multiple points of difference for each pair. (ayy mā huwa), and any differentia that behaves otherwise is merely a concomitant, not a constitutive differentia. The Five Universals
intermediate
10 min
Distinguishing the Five Universals by Their Differences
Author enumerates differences between each pair of the five universals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish each pair of the five universals (genus, species, differentia, property, general accident) by applying the specific criteria of essentiality, priority, extensivity, equality of predication, and convertibility as enumerated in the text.

Core Idea
The five universals are distinguished by their relations: essential vs accidental, prior vs posterior, one-species vs many, and converting vs non-converting.

In this passage, the author systematically lists the differences between each pair of the five universals. These differences are not merely for memorisation; they provide the analytical tools to identify which universal a given term is. By working through each comparison, you will learn to apply criteria such as constitutive vs non-constitutive, extensivity (how many species), equality of predication, and convertibility. These criteria are essential for definition theory and for evaluating arguments in Islamic sciences like usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Recall the five universals and their basic definitions
Write down the definitions: genus (jins) is predicated of many differing in species in answer to 'what is it?'; species (naw') is predicated of many differing only in number; differentia (fasl) is constitutive, predicated of many differing in species in answer to 'which is it?'; property (khassa) is a non-constitutive accident co-extensive with one species; general accident ('ard 'amm) is a non-constitutive accident applying to multiple species.
Refer to key_terms if needed.
2
Compare genus with property and general accident
From the passage: genus is prior in essence, encompasses multiple species, and is predicated equally. Property is posterior (arises with species), belongs to only one species. General accident may be predicated unequally (e.g., blackness varies in intensity) and exists in individuals as primary intention, while genus is secondary.
Notice the criteria: priority, extensivity, equality of predication, primary vs secondary intention.
3
Compare differentia with species, property, and general accident
Differentia is constitutive of the species, answers 'which is it?', is predicated of many differing in species, does not admit increase/decrease, and cannot have an opposite subject. Species answers 'what is it?', is predicated of many differing only in number. Property is non-constitutive but converts in predication (e.g., 'every human is rational' but not vice versa). General accident admits increase/decrease and can have opposites (e.g., white and black).
Check convertibility: property converts, differentia does not.
4
Compare species with property and general accident
Species is prior in existence and in estimation, answers 'what is it?', and applies equally to its subjects. Property is posterior, answers 'which is it?', and is unique to one species. General accident is posterior, may not apply equally (e.g., blackness in Negroes varies).
Priority and equality are key.
5
Compare property with general accident
Property exists only for one species, and sharing in it is equal. General accident applies to multiple species, and sharing may not be equal (e.g., 'white' applies to many but not equally).
This is the final pair: uniqueness vs multiplicity.

Source Text

والثالثة أن الجنس قبل النوع والنوع قبل العرض العام لكنها لا تكون مخصوصة بهما؛ إذ هي موجودة بين الجنس والخاصة أيضًا كما إذا قلت هذا ذاتي وذلك غير ذاتي مثلا. وأما بين الفصل والنوع فإن حمل النوع من طريق ما هو وحمل الفصل من طريق أي ما هو . فإن الإنسان وإن صلح أن يكون جوابًا عن أي حيوان هو فليس ذلك أولا بذاته؛ بل بسبب الناطق وقد بحث عن هذا من قبل. والثانية أن النوع الواحد قد يركب من الفصلين كما هو يذهب البعض، والفصل والنوع الواحد لا يمكن أن يركب من النوعين البتة لكن هذه المباينة تستمر مباينته على أحد الاعتبارين. والثالثة أن النوع لا يوجد البتة إلا وأن يكون محمولا على كثيرين مختلفين بالعدد فقط على خلاف الفصل فإنه يحمل على كثيرين مختلفين بالنوع، وهذه المباينة بين الفصل والنوع السافل . وأما بين الفصل والخاصة فالفصل مقوم للنوع دون الخاصة؛ إذ الخاصة وإن كانت مستلزمة للنوع فلا تكون مقومة البتة. ولأن الخاصة الحقيقية تنعكس في الحمل بخلاف الفصل فإنه قد لا ينعكس ، فلا يقال كل ناطق إنسان كما يقال كل إنسان ناطق. وأما بين الفصل والعرض العام فالطبيعية الفصلية لا تكون قابلة للزيادة والنقصان البتة بخلاف العرض العام؛ ولأن الشيء الواحد لا يمكن أن يكون موضوعا للفصل ولما يقابله كما في قولنا ناطق وغير ناطق ويمكن أن يكون موضوعًا للعرض العام. وأما بين النوع والخاصة فالنوع مقول في جواب ما هو دون الخاصة؛ إذ هي مقولة في جواب أي ما هو ؛ ولأن النوع متقدم في الوجود، والخاصة متأخرة لا محالة. وأما بين النوع والعرض العام فالنوع قبل العرض وجودًا وتوهما، والعرض لا يمكن أن يكون كذلك ؛ ولأن النوع يستوي لموضوعاته المشتركة فيه والعرض قد لا يستوي وإن كان غير مفارق كسواد الزنوج. وأما بين الخاصة والعرض العام فالخاصة لا توجد إلا لنوع واحد بخلاف العرض ؛ ولأن الاشتراك في العرض لا بحسب أن يكون بالسوية، وفي الخاصة بحسب فهذه هي المشاركات والمباينات المشهورة. وأما الغير فذلك يعرف بالتأمل إن شاء الله تعالى.
The second is that the genus is prior in essence and the property is posterior because it occurs with the occurrence of the species. The third is that the genus encompasses species while the property encompasses only one species. As for between the genus and the general accident: among them is that the genus is predicated of things under it only equally, while accidents are sometimes predicated unequally—for not all accidents are predicated unequally, like triangle and square for example. The second is that accidents exist in individuals as a primary intention, while species and genera do not exist as a primary intention. The third is that the genus is before the species and the species is before the general accident, but they are not specific to them because they exist between the genus and the property as well, as when you say 'this is essential' and 'that is accidental' for example. As for between the differentia and the species: the species is predicated as 'what it is' and the differentia is predicated as 'which it is'. For man, although he can be an answer to 'which animal is he?', that is not primarily by itself but because of 'rational'; this has been discussed previously. The second is that a single species may be composed of two differentiae as some hold, but a differentia and a single species cannot be composed of two species at all—yet this distinction persists according to one consideration. The third is that the species is never found except that it is predicated of many differing only in number, contrary to the differentia, for it is predicated of many differing in species—and this distinction is between the differentia and the lowest species. As for between the differentia and the property: the differentia is constitutive of the species, unlike the property; for although the property entails the species, it is not constitutive at all. And because the real property converts in predication, unlike the differentia, for it may not convert—so one does not say 'every rational is human' as one says 'every human is rational'. As for between the differentia and the general accident: the differential nature does not admit increase or decrease at all, unlike the general accident; and because one thing cannot be a subject for the differentia and its opposite, as in our saying 'rational' and 'non-rational', while it can be a subject for the general accident. As for between the species and the property: the species is said in answer to 'what is it?' unlike the property, for it is said in answer to 'which is it?'; and because the species is prior in existence while the property is necessarily posterior. As for between the species and the general accident: the species precedes the general accident in existence and in estimation, and the accident cannot be so; and because the species is equal to its subjects that share in it, while the accident may not be equal even if it is non-separating, like the blackness of Negroes. As for between the property and the general accident: the property exists only for one species, unlike the general accident; and because sharing in the general accident is not necessarily equal, while in the property it is. These are the famous commonalities and distinctions. As for others, they are known by reflection, God willing.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage enumerates the differences (mubayanat) between each pair of the five universals: genus (jins), species (naw'), differentia (fasl), property (khassa), and general accident ('ard 'amm). The author follows a systematic order. First, between genus and property: genus is prior in essence, encompasses multiple species, while property arises with the species and applies to only one species. Second, between genus and general accident: genus is always predicated equally of its subordinates, but accidents may be unequal (e.g., 'triangle' applies equally to all triangles, but some accidents like 'blackness' may vary in intensity); accidents exist in individuals as a primary intention (i.e., directly), while genera and species are secondary; also genus precedes species and species precedes general accident, but these temporal relations are not exclusive to these pairs. Third, between differentia and species: species answers 'what is it?', differentia answers 'which is it?'; a species can be composed of multiple differentiae, but a differentia cannot be composed of species; species is predicated of many differing in number only, while differentia is predicated of many differing in species (i.e., across species). Fourth, between differentia and property: differentia is constitutive (muqawwim) of the species, property is not; property converts in predication (e.g., 'every human is rational' and 'every rational is human'? Actually the text says the property converts but differentia does not: 'every human is rational' is true but 'every rational is human' is false because angels are rational, so the differentia does not convert). Fifth, between differentia and general accident: differential nature is not subject to increase/decrease, while general accident is; one thing cannot be subject to both a differentia and its opposite (e.g., cannot be both rational and non-rational), but can be subject to general accident. Sixth, between species and property: species answers 'what is it?', property answers 'which is it?'; species is prior in existence, property posterior. Seventh, between species and general accident: species precedes in existence and estimation; species applies equally to its subjects, general accident may not (e.g., blackness in Negroes is not equally intense). Eighth, between property and general accident: property is unique to one species, general accident applies to multiple; sharing in general accident is not necessarily equal, while in property it is. The passage concludes by noting that these are the well-known commonalities and distinctions, and others are to be known through reflection.

B) The property is constitutive of the species, while the differentia is not.
C) Both are constitutive of the species.
D) Neither is constitutive of the species.

Key Takeaway
The five universals are distinguished by a set of criteria: constitutive vs non-constitutive, prior vs posterior, one-species vs many, equal vs unequal predication, and convertibility; applying these criteria to each pair yields their specific differences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Relational Nature of the Five Universals
Author explains that the five universals are relational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why each of the five universals is defined relationally and provide an example from usul al-fiqh.

Core Idea
The five universals are not absolute; each is defined by its relation to the others.

This lesson introduces the concept of munasabat (relations) between the five universals. The author emphasizes that terms like genus, species, differentia, property, and accident are not fixed labels but are relative to each other. Understanding this relationality is crucial for precise definitions in Islamic sciences, where a term's logical role depends on what it is compared to.

Guided Path
1
Understand the key claim
The author states that the genus is not a genus for everything but only for its species. This means universals are functional, not absolute.
2
Identify the relational pattern
For each universal, ask: 'Relative to what is it defined?' Genus is relative to its species; species is relative to its genus.
3
Apply to differentia
Differentia is relative to a genus (as a divider) and to a species (as a constituent). It cannot exist independently.
4
Extend to property and accident
Property is relative to what accompanies its nature alone; accident is relative to what it accompanies, not absolutely.
5
Summarize the relational framework
All five universals are intelligible only through their mutual relations. This prevents treating them as fixed categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما «المناسبات» فالمناسبات بينها من الأمور التي يمكن أقسامها بالنظر إلى كل فرد من أفراد الخمسة التي مرَّ ذكرها ؛ وذلك لأن الجنس منها لا يكون جنسًا لكل شيء بل لنوعه فقط. وكذلك الفصل ليس إلا لجنس ما من حيث هو مقسم ولنوع ما من حيث هو مقوم وهو نوع ذلك الجنس. وكذلك في جنس الفصل وفصل الجنس وغيرهما ، فالجنس إذن إنما هو جنس بالقياس إلى الأمور التي يقال عليها في جواب ما هو، وهي أنواعه. وكذلك النوع إنما هو نوع بالقياس إلى الأمر الذي هو أعم منه وهو جنسه المتضمن لجميع ذاتياته التي يشاركه فيها الغير من الأنواع، والفصل أيضًا فصل بالقياس إلى ما يتميز به في ذاته والخاصة خاصة بالقياس إلى ما يعرض لطبيعته وحده. وكذلك العرض العام فإنه بالقياس إلى ما يعرض له لا مطلقًا؛ بل إذا أخذ
As for 'the relations' (al-munasabat): the relations between them are among the things whose divisions can be considered with respect to each individual of the five that have been mentioned. That is because the genus among them is not a genus for everything but only for its species. Likewise, the differentia is only for a certain genus insofar as it is a divisor and for a certain species insofar as it is a constituent, and it is a species of that genus. And similarly for the genus of the differentia, the differentia of the genus, and others. Thus, the genus is only a genus in relation to the things of which it is said in answer to 'what is it?', and they are its species. Likewise, the species is only a species in relation to that which is more general than it, which is its genus that encompasses all its essentials in which other species share. And the differentia is also a differentia in relation to that by which it is distinguished in its essence. And the property is a property in relation to what accompanies its nature alone. And similarly, the general accident is in relation to what it accompanies, not absolutely; rather, when it is taken...
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of munasabat (relations/appropriateness) between the five universals. It explains that each of the five is not an absolute term but is understood relatively. The genus is genus only in relation to its species (the things that answer 'what is it?' under it). The differentia is a differentia relative to a certain genus (as a divider) and to a certain species (as a constituent). Similarly, a species is a species relative to its genus (the more general that contains its essentials). The differentia is also relative to what it distinguishes in essence. The property is property relative to what accompanies its nature alone (i.e., uniquely). The general accident is relative to what it accompanies, not in an absolute sense. Unfortunately, the text cuts off before completing the explanation, but the key point is that these terms are relational.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Distinctions between Species, Property, and Common Accident
Author explains the differences among species, property, and common accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish species, property, and common accident based on predication type, existential priority, uniformity, and exclusivity.

Core Idea
Species answers 'what is it?'; property answers 'what sort is it?'; common accident spans multiple species.

In the study of the five universals, understanding the differences between species (naw'), property (khassa), and common accident ('arad 'amm) is crucial for correct definition. This passage from al-Mantiq al-Kabir lays out three pairwise comparisons, each highlighting a distinct logical feature. By mastering these distinctions, you will sharpen your ability to analyze definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three universals compared
Locate the terms 'النوع' (species), 'الخاصة' (property), and 'العرض العام' (common accident) in the text.
2
Note the criteria of comparison
The author uses four criteria: type of predication ('what is it?' vs 'what sort is it?'), priority in existence, uniformity among subjects, and exclusivity to a single species.
3
Work through each pairwise comparison
First comparison: species vs property – species answers 'what is it?' and is prior; property answers 'what sort is it?' and is posterior. Second: species vs common accident – species is prior in existence and estimation and uniform; common accident is not prior and may not be uniform (e.g., blackness of Zanj varies). Third: property vs common accident – property belongs to one species and is shared equally; common accident spans multiple species and may not be equal.
Pay attention to the example of 'blackness of the Zanj' illustrating non-uniformity.
4
Summarize the differences in a table
Create a table with columns for each universal and rows for each criterion (predication, priority, uniformity, exclusivity). Fill in based on the text.
This will help you internalize the distinctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بين النوع والخاصة فالنوع مقول في جواب ما هو دون الخاصة؛ إذ هي مقولة في جواب أي ما هو ; ولأن النوع متقدم في الوجود، والخاصة متأخرة لا محالة. وأما بين النوع والعرض العام فالنوع قبل العرض وجودًا وتوهما، والعرض لا يمكن أن يكون كذلك ; ولأن النوع يستوي لموضوعاته المشتركة فيه والعرض قد لا يستوي وإن كان غير مفارق كسواد الزنوج. وأما بين الخاصة والعرض العام فالخاصة لا توجد إلا لنوع واحد بخلاف العرض ; ولأن الاشتراك في العرض لا بحسب أن يكون بالسوية، وفي الخاصة بحسب فهذه هي المشاركات والمباينات المشهورة. وأما الغير فذلك يعرف بالتأمل إن شاء الله تعالى.
And as for between the species and the property, the species is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' without the property, while the property is predicated in answer to 'what sort is it?'; and because the species is prior in existence, and the property is necessarily posterior. And as for between the species and the common accident, the species is before the accident in existence and estimation, and the accident cannot be like that; because the species is uniform among its common subjects, while the accident may not be uniform even if it is inseparable, like the blackness of the Zanj people. And as for between the property and the common accident, the property does not exist except for one species, unlike the accident; because sharing in the accident is not necessarily equal, while in the property it is equal. So these are the well-known commonalities and differences. And as for others, that is known by reflection, God willing.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of the distinctions among three of the five universals: species (naw'), property (khassa), and common accident ('arad 'amm). The author proceeds comparison by comparison. First, between species and property: the species is predicated in answer to 'what is it?' (ma huwa), while property answers 'what sort is it?' (ayy ma huwa). Moreover, the species is prior in existence, whereas property is posterior. Second, between species and common accident: the species is prior both in existence and in the mind, while the accident cannot be so. Also, the species is uniform across its individuals, but an accident like the blackness of the Zanj people is not uniform since it admits variation. Third, between property and common accident: property belongs exclusively to one species, while common accident spans multiple species. Additionally, property is shared equally by all individuals of that species, whereas common accident may not be shared equally. The author concludes that these are the well-known relations, and other distinctions require further reflection.

Key Terms
النوع
الخاصة
العرض العام

Key Takeaway
The well-known differences among species, property, and common accident are based on predication type, existential priority, uniformity, and exclusivity to a single species.
